1.       INTRODUCTION

1.1.     Background
1.       Hai Phong City is Viet Nam’s principal seaport and one of the five centrally administered
cities. It lies in the Red River Delta about 100 km east of Ha Noi and serves as the industrial and
logistics gateway of Northern Viet Nam. Rapid economic growth and urban expansion over the past
20 years have exerted strong pressure on urban drainage, wastewater, and transport systems.
2.      Recurrent flooding occurs in the low-lying districts of Hong Bang, Le Chan, An Duong and
Ngo Quyen because the existing river channels (Re, Tam Bac and Lach Tray) are silted, the pumping
capacity is insufficient, and the tidal influence from Lach Huyen Estuary restricts drainage. Untreated
domestic wastewater is still discharged directly into open canals, causing deterioration of water
quality and sanitary conditions.
3.      Climate-change projections from MONRE (2023) indicate sea-level rise of 0.26–0.34 m by
2050 and more frequent extreme rainfall events. Hai Phong ranks among the most climate-vulnerable
coastal cities in Viet Nam and was listed by OECD (2016) as one of 13 port cities worldwide facing
the highest potential economic losses from sea-level rise by 2070.
4.     To cope with these challenges, the Government of Viet Nam, with financial and technical
support from the World Bank, is preparing the Hai Phong Resilient and Sustainable Urban
Development Project (HPRSDP). The Project supports implementation of the National Climate
Change Strategy (Decision 896/QĐ-TTg, 2022), the National Green Growth Strategy (Decision
1658/QĐ-TTg, 2021), and the Hai Phong City Master Plan to 2040 (Vision 2050) approved by
Decision 323/QĐ-TTg (2023).
5.     The Project aims to build an integrated system for flood protection, drainage, wastewater
treatment, and transport resilience that will strengthen the city’s adaptive capacity and improve
environmental quality for approximately 1.5 million residents.
1.2.     Project Objectives
1.2.1. General Objective
6.      The Project Development Objective (PDO) is to enhance climate resilience, environmental
sustainability, and urban livability in Hai Phong City through integrated flood-risk management,
wastewater collection and treatment, and resilient transport infrastructure.
1.2.2. Specific objectives
7.       Specific objectives are to:
     ➢   Reduce urban flooding and tidal inundation through rehabilitation of Re and Tam Bac Rivers,
         construction of sluice gates and pumping stations, and improvement of the city’s drainage
         network;
     ➢   Improve water-environment quality by expanding wastewater collection and treatment,
         thereby reducing pollutant loads discharged to surface waters;
     ➢   Strengthen urban connectivity and transport resilience by upgrading key corridors such as
         Ring Road No. 3 and associated bridges;
     ➢   Promote institutional capacity building for integrated flood-risk and environmental
         management consistent with the World Bank Environmental and Social Framework (ESF).



                                                                                             Page | 1
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

1.3.   Project’s components
8.       The HPRSDP consists of four inter-related components combining physical investments and
institutional support.
Component 1 – Thuy Nguyen Climate-Resilient Urban Infrastructure Development (Ring
Road No. 3)
9.      This component implements a key regional corridor connecting the northern urban area of
Hai Phong (Thuy Nguyen District) with the Hai Phong city center and the Hanoi – Hai Phong
Expressway. It will promote socio-economic integration and support safe urban expansion toward
the north bank of Cam River. Main works include:
  ➢    Construction of Ring Road No. 3: total length ≈ 18.65 km (from Km0 at the intersection
       with Hanoi – Hai Phong Expressway to Km18 + 650 at National Highway 10). The cross-
       section width is 68 m (planned), implemented in Phase 1 with a 27 m carriageway. Design
       speed 80 km/h.
  ➢    Bridges and interchanges: Construction of Ngo Quyen Bridge and Ruot Lon Bridge
       crossing Cam and Ruot Lon Rivers (total length ≈ 2.67 km), category I and special-grade
       bridges with reinforced concrete structure.
  ➢    Drainage and lighting system, green belts and safety corridors synchronized with the
       road.
10.    This component will significantly improve regional connectivity and facilitate resilient urban
development in Thuy Nguyen.
Component 2 – Re River Environmental Improvement and Wastewater System (An Duong 1
WWTP and Pumping Stations)
11.     This component aims to restore and protect the Re River as a strategic urban waterway by
improving its environmental condition and enhancing wastewater collection and treatment capacity
for the western urban area. Key works include:
  ➢    River embankment and management roads: Rehabilitation of approximately 9.5 km of
       riverbanks (4.7 km left bank, 4.8 km right bank) with revetment slopes and vertical
       reinforced-concrete walls combined with management roads (7–9 m wide).
  ➢    Drainage and wastewater collection network: Construction of interceptor sewers (D400–
       D2000) along both banks of Re River and secondary sewer branches within An Duong
       District and adjacent areas (total ≈ 15 km).
  ➢    Pumping stations: three stations (PS-01 to PS-03) with individual capacities of 5,000–
       15,000 m³/day each, transmitting wastewater to the central treatment plant.
  ➢    Construction of An Duong 1 Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP): capacity of 35,000
       m³/day (as applied in ESIA; Phase 1 in FS = 10,000 m³/day). The plant is located in An Dong
       commune, An Duong district, occupying 6.5 ha; treatment technology: combined biological
       process (A2/O – secondary sedimentation – disinfection) meeting QCVN 14:2008/BTNMT,
       Column A before discharge to Re River.
12.    The component will directly benefit about 215,000 residents in An Duong and adjacent urban
wards by reducing pollution and improving the urban water environment.
Component 3 – Central Urban Flood Control and Water Environment Improvement (Tam Bac
– Lach Tray System)



                                                                                           Page | 2
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

13.   This component addresses flood risks in the central urban core (Hong Bang, Ngo Quyen, Le
Chan, Hai An districts) through structural measures on the Tam Bac and Lach Tray river systems.
Main works include:
 ➢     Tam Bac regulating sluice and floodgate complex: new reinforced concrete gates with
       automated control to manage tidal inflows and urban runoff.
 ➢     Thuy Doi sluice and drainage pumping station: capacity ≈ 12 m³/s for storm water
       discharge into Lach Tray River.
 ➢     Rehabilitation of existing canals and box culverts: total length ≈ 7.8 km (D800–B3000),
       connecting urban wards to receiving rivers.
 ➢     Roads and embankments along Tam Bac River: improvement of 4.3 km urban
       embankment with pedestrian paths, green spaces, and lighting.
14.    These measures will reduce flooding duration and extent in the city core by 40–60 %, protect
approximately 3,500 ha of urban area, and improve water quality and amenities along Tam Bac and
Lach Tray Rivers.
Component 4 – Systems and Capacity Development for Climate-Resilient Urban Management
15.    This component focuses on enhancing institutional capacity, data management and smart
urban governance to ensure effective implementation and operation of resilient infrastructure.
 ➢     Development of an Integrated Flood and Drainage Management Information System
       (GIS & SCADA platform for hydraulic operations and early warning).
 ➢     Establishment of a Climate Resilience and Urban Management Center under Hai Phong
       City People’s Committee.
 ➢     Capacity-building programs for urban planning, environmental management and disaster risk
       reduction.




                        Figure 1. General layout of project components
                                                                                         Page | 3
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)


2.     OBJECTIVES AND METHODOLOGY OF SOCIAL ASSESSMENT

2.1.   Objectives
16.     The social assessment is conducted simultaneously with the project's environmental impact
assessment with two objectives. First, it examines potential positive and adverse impacts of the
project based on planned project activities. Second, findings in the report provide information for
designing measures to address identified potential adverse impacts and propose community
development activities relevant to the project's development objectives. Identifying unavoidable
negative impacts should consult local people, government agencies, project stakeholders, etc.
17.     During the assessment, if any negative impacts occur as a consequence of the project,
appropriate measures must be implemented immediately (before project implementation) to avoid,
minimize, and mitigate these negative impacts, or if unavoidable, compensation will be provided to
affected persons.
18.   The task will comply with Vietnam Government laws and policies, and relevant World Bank
ESF and ESSs.
2.2.   Scope of work and assessment
19.    Four tasks were undertaken to prepare the Social Assessment (SA) including: (i) Socio-
economic profile: (ii) Stakeholders analysis; (iii) Public Consultation and Participation; and (iv)
Socio-economic baseline surveys.
20.     Social Assessment (SA) is a process to ensure that project activities will (i) include major
relevant social issues and develop mitigation measures, and (ii) integrate multi-stakeholder
engagement strategies. Social assessment is an iterative process and must be conducted multiple
times in several project cycle phases. The figure below illustrates an overview of the typical social
assessment process/steps.




                              Figure 2. Social Assessment Process.
2.2.1. General Principles
21.    The following principles are used to ensure the quality of the study:
  ❖    Bottom-up approach principle: This enhances and ensures broad participation from various
       Bottom-up approach principal communities, including poor households. It enables the Project
       to hear their voices and understand the desires and recommendations of diverse social groups.
  ❖    Combining qualitative and quantitative data principle.

                                                                                           Page | 4
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

 ❖     Cross-checking: Collected information is always verified across multiple sources to ensure
       reasonableness and accuracy.
 ❖     Field verification: Field checks are employed to confirm the reasonableness and accuracy of
       information.
22.    The following methods and techniques will be used to gather information and conduct the
evaluation:
2.2.2. Secondary Document Research
23.     Reviewing and analyzing project-related documents provides baseline information about the
project and explains why changes are occurring or not. It also identifies data gaps requiring further
collection and assessment. Relevant project data is sourced from the Project Management Board and
local socio-economic analyses, such as the Hai Phong Statistical Yearbook and commune/ward
socio-economic reports in the project area.
2.2.3. Quantitative Methods
24.    After reviewing project documents and reconnaissance, the survey framework and methods
were discussed to determine suitable sample sizes and data collection techniques. Two primary
survey techniques were selected:
 ❖     Using forms/templates among functional agencies of project wards/communes;
 ❖     Household Socio-Economic Survey, December 2025 via structured questionnaires during
       Resettlement Action Plan preparation. This requires a sampling strategy for pre- and post-
       project evaluation. As per the TOR, surveys target potentially affected
       households/beneficiaries, documenting the project-affected area, current social conditions,
       and trends, including local authorities (city/ward/communal department representatives) and
       households (beneficiaries, vulnerable groups, at-risk families, varying living standards
25.    As noted in the TOR, surveys cover potentially affected households/beneficiaries, providing
data on the project area and social trends, including:
 ❖     Local authorities: Representatives from city, ward/commune project-area departments;
 ❖     Households: Beneficiaries, vulnerable households, project-impacted families, those with
       diverse living standards.
26.    A stratified random sampling survey collected data from affected communities and
beneficiaries using two household lists:
 ❖     First list: Residents in selected study areas, based on project investment maps. Consultants
       collaborate with local officials (ward/commune statistical staff or residential group leaders)
       to compile lists.
 ❖     Samples account for residential geography and specific components, proportionally including
       street-side households, neighborhood residents, those adjacent to investments, and near land
       acquisition zones.
 ❖     Second list: Households affected by each investment component, compiled from basic
       designs by consultants, project communes' staff, and provincial Project Management Board's
       land officers.
27.    The Socio-Economic Survey, December 2025 was carried out within one month from October
8, /2025 to November 8, 2025 with 463 households who are consulted through a questionnaire
covering 08 wards of Hai Phong City. The number of households participating in the survey are
shown in the table below:


                                                                                           Page | 5
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

28.     Direct community consultation meetings: A total of 16 consultation meetings were organized
in 11 out of 13 wards within the Project area (remaining Thuy Nguyen and Ngo Quyen wards will be
organized during the later stage due to the absence of residential populations), with 1,917 participants
(1,145 male and 772 female). The feedback collected was incorporated into the ESIA, RP, and the
Project’s environmental and social management plans.
29.    Online consultation: The Project Owner issued Official Letter No. 1593/BQL-DA1 dated 01
October 2025 to the Ministry of Agriculture and Environment (MAE) to conduct consultation
through public disclosure on the electronic information portal for a period of 15 days, in accordance
with regulations.
30.   Additionally, an Inventory of Losses (IOL) survey was conducted using 2,652 forms for
households affected by land acquisition for the Project.
31.     Focus Group Discussions (FGDs): 11 FGDs were conducted in 11 wards; focusing on
livelihoods, social impacts, gender, and vulnerable groups.
32.    In-depth Interviews (KIIs): 11 in-depth interviews were conducted. Target groups included:
severely affected households, vulnerable households, representatives of local authorities, business
households along the drainage and wastewater collection routes, and fishing households on the Re
River.
2.2.4. Qualitative Methods
33.    Qualitative surveys aim to gather information that structured questionnaires (quantitative
methods) cannot fully cover. The data obtained answers "why" and "how" questions, providing in-
depth explanations of phenomena or behaviors. Additionally, qualitative information explores
thoughts, emotions, attitudes, and aspirations of respondents, particularly on sensitive issues.
Qualitative surveys are conducted through focus group discussions and in-depth interviews. The
communes selected for this method are the same areas as those in the quantitative survey above.
34.     Consultation in the form of sending written requests for comments. The Project Management
Board has sent copies and drafts Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) to 12 affected
units/organizations (including: An Hai Port Company Limited, VNT Logistics Company Limited,
VIDIFI Duyen Hai Joint Stock Company, Nam Hai Maritime Company Limited, Le Lai Service Joint
Stock Company, An Duong Fine Arts Cooperative, Sakura Film Studio, Minh Tien Trading and
Service Company Limited, Dong Dat Company Limited, Hai Phong Forest Protection Department,
An Hai Irrigation Works Exploitation Company Limited, Vietnam Infrastructure Development and
                                                                                              Page | 6
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Financial Investment Corporation) and 13 local authorities at ward/commune level in the project area
to request consultation on the content of the drafts.
35.     Consultation in the form of direct meetings. The PMU and consulting units organized 11 (for
ESIA) and 14 (for RP) community consultation meetings in 11/13 wards in the project area. In
particular, Thuy Nguyen and Ngo Quyen wards have absolutely no residents living in the project area
and the land has been cleared, so no meetings were organized. Summary information about the
consultation meetings is presented in stakeholder engagement plan (SEP).
36.      Issues discussed during public consultations included: (i) Introducing project investment
components; (ii) Overview of local socio-economic situation; (iii) Current residential infrastructure
status, including technical infrastructure (electricity, roads) and social infrastructure (schools, health
stations); (iv) Local infrastructure construction and upgrading needs; (v) Screening/Assessing
potential impacts during construction that may affect the socio-economic and cultural aspects of
residents in the project area.
2.2.5. Direct Observation
37.     This method helps obtain timely and useful information to supplement the collected data,
better understand the foundation of the gathered information and data, and explain the survey results.
2.2.6. Data Processing and Analysis
38.    Responses from structured questionnaires (structured interviews) are processed using SPSS.
Qualitative information is handled in two ways: (i) Using on-site direct techniques if available; (ii)
Quotes and case studies are used to generate clearer quantitative insights.
2.2.7. Process of Socio-Economic Survey, December 2025

3.        NATURAL, SOCIO-ECONOMIC CONDITIONS OF THE PROJECT
          AREA

3.1.      Natural Characteristics
       Geographical Location
43.     Haiphong is a coastal city in the Red River Delta, one of Vietnam's six centrally governed
municipalities, ranking as the third largest city and a vital port hub that serves as the industrial,
maritime, economic, cultural, medical, educational, scientific, commercial, and technological center
of the Northern Coastal Region. It holds Type I urban status as both a regional and national central
city. Geographically, it borders Quang Ninh Province to the north, Hung Yen Province to the south,
and the Gulf of Tonkin (South China Sea) to the east, situated approximately 70 km from Bach Long
Vi Island (an outlying district of Haiphong) and 106 km east-southeast of Hanoi via National
Highway 5.
44.    The Haiphong City Climate Change Adaptation Development Project spans the
administrative boundaries of Dong Hai Ward, Nam Trieu Ward, Bach Dang Ward, Hoa Binh Ward,
Thuy Nguyen Ward, Luu Kiem Ward, Hong Bang Ward, An Duong Ward, An Hai Ward, Ngo Quyen
Ward, Gia Vien Ward, Le Chan Ward, and An Bien Ward.
       Topography


                                                                                                Page | 8
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

45.     Haiphong's topography and geomorphology exhibit significant diversity, resulting from
prolonged geological processes over hundreds of millions of years combined with sediment
deposition from the Red River and Thai Binh River systems. It can be divided into two primary zones:
the northern part features a plain interspersed with low hills and mountains, while the southern part
comprises a purely flat lowland plain; offshore archipelagos such as Cat Ba and Bach Long Vy are
also present.
46.      Inland hills and mountains in Haiphong average 50-100 m in elevation, covering 15% of the
city's total area and distributed sporadically across the northern sector in continuous or intermittent
bands oriented northwest-southeast. The pure lowland plain encompasses An Duong, Tien Lang, and
Vinh Bao Districts, as well as much of Kien An and Do Son Districts, with absolute elevations
ranging from 2.5 m to 3.5 m, gradually decreasing from west to east. The surface is predominantly
covered by silty clay, sandy loam, and alluvial deposits, featuring numerous ponds, lakes, marshes,
and tidal-flooded lowlands along major rivers such as Van Uc, Lach Tray, Thai Binh, and Hoa Rivers.
Notably, Haiphong boasts over 125 km of coastline with multiple large river mouths, leading to
substantial annual sediment discharge that forms expansive sandy-muddy spits extending seaward by
several kilometers in places, developing mangrove forests or reclaimed economic zones like Tran
Duong (Vinh Bao), Vinh Quang, Tien Hung, Dong Hung, Tay Hung (Tien Lang), Tan Trao (Kien
Thuy), and Dinh Vu areas.
47.    These characteristics significantly influence the riverine flow regime: water convergence
occurs more rapidly in limestone and low hill areas than in plains, while tidal lowlands experience
frequent inundation; the northwest-southeast topographic gradient facilitates seaward tidal ebb flows.
48.     The project area features relatively flat terrain, primarily traversing existing agricultural land
and intersected by two major rivers, Cam River and Ruot Lon River.
 ❖     (1) Ring Road 3 section: Mainly through lowland plains and rice fields with elevations
       ranging from 0.01-4.5 m; the initial segment connects to the existing Bui Vien Road, linking
       to the Hanoi-Haiphong Expressway up to the Cam River bank (Ngo Quyen Bridge area), then
       crossing to Vu Yen Island under urban development with flat terrain, subsequently passing
       Ruot Lon River through rice paddies, aquaculture ponds, and existing residential areas in
       Bach Dang, Nam Trieu, Hoa Binh, and Luu Kiem Wards.
 ❖     (2) Wastewater treatment plant site: Primarily rice fields and natural ponds with elevations of
       +0.8 to +1.1 m; access roads to the plant are at approximately +2.50 m.
 ❖     (3) Along Re River: Flat terrain with farmland on both banks and adjacent residential areas;
       riverbed elevation is about -1.8 m
 ❖     (4) Regulating gate, stormwater, and wastewater culvert sites: Central urban areas with flat,
       low-lying terrain are prone to flooding during heavy rain or high tides.
     Hydrology
49.     River discharges exhibit substantial variation and seasonal fluctuations. Flow distribution
ratios among rivers have shifted over time: for the Luoc-Thai Binh system, they comprised 30% from
1960-1967, declined to 25% during 1968-1972, and subsequently decreased for the Thai Binh River
while increasing for the Moi River.
50.    River flows display pronounced seasonal changes, aligning with rainy (flood) and dry
seasons, where floods typically lag rainfall by one month (June-October) and dry periods extend from
November to May of the following year. Flood-season discharge constitutes 75-85% of annual totals,
with July-September alone accounting for 50-70%; peak floods generally occur in July or August
(20-27%, occasionally up to 35% of annual flow). Northern rivers (Bach Dang, Van Uc, Lach Tray)

                                                                                               Page | 9
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

experience stronger influence from the Thai Binh flood regime, whereas southern rivers (Luoc, Hoa,
Thai Binh, Moi) are more affected by Red River floods; during dry seasons, upstream inflows are
minimal, with river water primarily derived from groundwater and tides, representing only 15-20%
of annual discharge.
51.     Annual minimum flows typically occur in March: Kinh Thay River (Cam River Mouth
station) averages 115 m³/s in the dry season (minimum 47.2 m³/s); Van Uc River (Trung Trang
station) averages 193 m³/s (minimum 63.2 m³/s); Moi River (Moi River station) averages 82.6 m³/s
(minimum 53.0 m³/s); Thai Binh River (Cong Ro station) averages 16.4 m³/s (minimum 1.1 m³/s).
3.2.      Land Resources and Population
       Land Resources
52.     Pursuant to the National Assembly Resolution establishing the new Haiphong City through
merger with Hai Duong Province, the city now features a restructured administration under the name
“Haiphong City” encompassing 114 commune-level units (67 communes, 45 wards, and 2 special
zones).
53.   Following the July 2025 merger, Haiphong's total population reaches 4,664,124, with Bach
Long Vi recording the smallest at 686 residents and Le Chan Ward the largest at 161,051.
54.    The former Haiphong City covered 15 districts with a total natural land area of 152,643.6 ha,
including 48,146.6 ha agricultural (31.54%), 6,320.8 ha forestry (11.89%), 10,918.68 ha specialized
use (22.14%), and 15,556.9 ha residential (10.19%).
       Population
55.    Post-July 2025 merger, Haiphong's total population stands at 4,664,124, with Bach Long Vi
having the fewest inhabitants at 686 and Le Chan Ward the most at 161,051.

4.        SOCIO-ECONOMIC CHARACTERISTICS IN THE PROJECT AREA

4.1.       General Characteristics
56.    Economic Overview (First Half of 2025) Hai Phong City continues to demonstrate robust
economic resilience and growth, maintaining its position as a leading economic hub in the Red River
Delta and Vietnam.
  ❖       GRDP Growth: The Gross Regional Domestic Product (GRDP) is estimated at over VND
          209,600 billion, reflecting an 11.2% increase-on-year (1.5 times the national average). Hai
          Phong ranks 2nd among 34 provinces/cities and 1st among centrally governed cities.
  ❖       Industrial Production: The Industrial Production Index (IIP) surged by 14.6%, significantly
          outperforming the national growth rate of 9.2%.
  ❖       Budget & Investment: Total state budget revenue exceeded VND 100,000 billion. Realized
          investment capital reached over VND 139,800 billion (up nearly 19% year-on-year).
  ❖       FDI & Tourism: The city attracted approximately USD 1.3 billion in FDI and welcomed over
          7.5 million tourist arrivals (a 28% increase).
57.    The project area covers a diverse mix of administrative units, ranging from highly urbanized
wards with strong trade/services to peri-urban areas with significant agricultural activities.
  ❖       Budget Performance: Most wards reported strong fiscal health, meeting or exceeding revenue
          targets for the first 9 months of 2025. Notable performers include Dong Hai (155.6% of
          estimate), An Bien (156%), and Le Chan (131%).

                                                                                          P a g e | 10
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

 ❖    Trade, Services, and Industry:
         o      High Activity Zones: Wards like An Duong, Hoa Binh, and Luu Kiem host substantial
                numbers of enterprises and business households (e.g., An Duong has over 2,500
                business households; Hoa Binh has over 3,400).
         o      Employment: The service and industrial sectors provide stable employment. For
                instance, in Luu Kiem, the structure is 32% industry, 37% commerce, and 31%
                services.
 ❖    Agriculture and Aquaculture (Key Livelihoods in Peri-urban Wards):
         o      Nam Trieu: A major hub for fisheries with 366 ha of aquaculture (annual output
                ~3,200 tons) and 377 fishing vessels (output ~61,000 tons).
         o      Thuy Nguyen & Hoa Binh: Maintain significant aquaculture activities (Thuy Nguyen:
                79 ha; Hoa Binh: 214 ha).
         o      Crop Production: Wards like An Hai, An Duong, and Luu Kiem maintain large rice
                cultivation areas (e.g., Luu Kiem: ~1,045 ha).
58.   Social Security and Poverty Reduction:
 ❖    Poverty Status: The project area is characterized by very low poverty rates, indicating
      effective local social security nets.
         o      No Poor Households: Recorded in Thuy Nguyen, Ngo Quyen, and Hoa Binh wards.
         o      Near-poor Households: Rates are minimal (e.g., An Hai: 0.47%; Hong Bang: 5
                households; Hoa Binh: 0.58%).
 ❖    Social Assistance: All wards implement timely support for policy beneficiaries, especially
      during holidays. An Bien and Le Chan are active in providing monthly allowances and health
      insurance cards for the elderly and vulnerable groups.
59.   Education and Health:
 ❖    Education: Universalization of education is achieved across the board.
         o      Enrollment: 100% enrollment rates for proper age groups in preschool, primary, and
                secondary levels (e.g., An Hai, An Duong, Ngo Quyen, Hong Bang).
         o      Facilities: Continuous investment in school infrastructure to meet national standards
                (e.g., Hoa Binh has 100% Level 1 National Standard schools).
 ❖    Health:
         o      Insurance Coverage: High health insurance participation rates (e.g., An Hai: 97.5%;
                Ngo Quyen: 95.8%; Hong Bang: 100%).
         o      Services: Local health stations function effectively in primary care, vaccination
                (EPI), and disease prevention (Dengue, Rabies, etc.).
60.   Infrastructure and Environment:
 ❖    Basic Services:
         o      Water & Sanitation: Urban wards like Ngo Quyen, Dong Hai, and Hoa Binh report
                100% access to clean water and 100% solid waste collection/treatment.
         o      Electricity/Lighting: 100% coverage in main roads and residential areas (e.g., Dong
                Hai).



                                                                                           P a g e | 11
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

 ❖       Digital Transformation: Wards like An Bien, Gia Vien, and Hong Bang are aggressively
         implementing digital administration, with high rates of online document processing and
         digital signatures.
61.     The survey team conducted a socio-economic assessment in 13 communes/wards hosting
project components in Haiphong City, involving 463 households (sample sizes varied by investment
scope per commune; see Table 1 for household survey details).
     Gender of Respondents
62.     Survey results indicate higher male participation: among 463 interviewees, 283 were male
(61.12%) and 180 female (38.88%). This reflects traditional local cultural characteristics where men
typically register as household heads and represent the family insocial relations. However, the nearly
39% participation rate of women indicates their significant role in family decision-making. This
disparity necessitates specific measures in subsequent consultation steps to ensure women's voices
are fully heard.

65.      Survey respondents predominantly fall into the 56+ age group (74.47.3%), followed by 46-
55 years (16.39%), 36-45 years (6.91%), and a minimal proportion in 18-25 years (0.35%).
Consequently, participants are primarily household heads or senior family members, who typically
hold decision-making authority and influence within families, enabling reliable assessments of local
socio-economic issues and active engagement in project activities. However, this age structure (with
over 90% of respondents aged over 46) presents a challenge for the Livelihood Restoration Program
(LRP), as vocational training solutions for career transition may prove ineffective. Consequently,
support measures should focus on maintaining existing production models or facilitating access to
capital.
    Relationship with Household Head
66.     The majority of respondents are household heads (299 individuals, 64.58%), followed by
spouses (27%), with the remainder being adult children over 18 (7.56%). This composition enhances
the accuracy and reliability of questionnaire responses. However, the low participation rate of adult
children (under 8%) indicates a potential information gap regarding the younger workforce. While
household heads provide accurate data on assets, they may not fully reflect the employment
aspirations and training needs of the younger generation. Therefore, during the implementation
phase—especially for activities related to local recruitment—the Project Management Unit (PMU)
should establish specific channels to engage this youth group.

4.3.      Classification from Survey
80.    Economic development varies across project areas, leading to differing household prosperity
perceptions; a prosperous household in one locale may qualify as average elsewhere, underscoring
enumerators' critical role in classification based on these criteria:
  ❖     MOLISA urban poverty standards (2021-2025): poor <2.000.000 VND/person/month; near-
        poor 2,000,000-3,000,000; better-off (affluent/rich) >3,000,000 VND/person/month.
  ❖     Enumerator observations of housing and amenities during interviews.
81.    Consultants recommend: poor (including near-poor) ≤2,000,000 VND/person/month;
average 2,000,001-3,000,000; better-off >3,000,000 VND/person/month.
4.3.1. Classification from Survey
82.     Vulnerable groups—those with inferior political, economic, or social standing relative to
others, prone to oversight or rights violations—warrant heightened protection. Identifying these
groups is pivotal to serving as a basis for compensation policies, resettlement plans, and livelihood
restoration strategies. Based on the results of the Inventory of Losses (IOL), the Project has identified
households belonging to these groups to design appropriate support measures.
83.     Aggregated data from the project areas indicates that Social Policy Households constitute the
largest group with 81 households, concentrated mainly in Component 1 (60 households) and

                                                                                              P a g e | 16
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Component 2 (21 households). This group typically includes families with meritorious services to
the revolution or those receiving regular social welfare allowances, requiring prioritization during
public consultation.
84.     Ranking second is the group of Female-headed households (with dependents) with 49
households (comprising 42 households in Component 1 and 7 households in Component 2). This
demographic requires specific attention regarding gender equality and tailored livelihood support to
ensure they are not disproportionately impacted.
85.    Additionally, the project recorded 35 households with persons with disabilities and 10 poor
households being affected. Notably, all 10 affected poor households are located exclusively within
the scope of Component 1 (Hoa Binh, Bach Dang, Nam Trieu wards), while Component 2 recorded
no affected poor households regarding land acquisition. These poor and disabled households will be
considered top priority beneficiaries for the Income Restoration Program (IRP).
4.3.2. Household size
86.      The Socio-Economic Survey, December 2025 was conducted covering a total of 463
households. The survey results regarding average household size show that: The Dong Hai area has
the largest household size with an average of 6.0 persons/household; however, it should be noted that
the sample size here is very small (only 3 households), resulting in low representativeness. Nam Trieu
follows with an average of 5.05 persons/household (a large sample of 98 households), indicating that
this is an area with a tradition of large or multi-generational families.
87.     Implications for Resettlement: The average household size in areas such as Nam Trieu, Bach
Dang, and Hoa Binh ranges from 4.7 to over 5 persons/household, peaking at 10-11 members. This
reflects the prevalence of multi-generational families. This is a critical factor for resettlement
planning. For large households (over 8 members), a standard resettlement plot may not suffice.
Consequently, the project must carefully consider mechanisms for household splitting verification or
allocating supplementary resettlement slots for multiple couples cohabiting within a single household
to avoid overcrowding and ensure living standards post-resettlement.
88.     Vulnerable Groups: The presence of single-person households (Min = 1) across most wards
indicates a potential demographic of elderly people living alone. This group requires careful
identification during the Detailed Measurement Survey (DMS) as they are likely eligible for special
social welfare assistance beyond standard compensation policies.
89.    Urbanization Trends: There is a clear divergence in household size. Highly urbanized areas
like Hong Bang and An Duong exhibit smaller average sizes (~4 persons), reflecting a nuclear family
trend, whereas peri-urban areas retain larger family structures. Livelihood restoration programs
should be tailored accordingly: prioritizing industrial/service jobs for nuclear families, while
leveraging the abundant labor force of larger families for household-based economic models.

4.3.3. Age of household heads
90.     A total of 463 household heads participated in the survey. Overall Mean Age: The average
age of household heads is 62 years. This indicates that the household heads in the survey sample are
primarily elderly or retired individuals. Age Range: The youngest household head is 24 years old,
and the oldest is 92 years old.
 ❖     Highest: Nam Trieu (66.6 years) – This is the area with the oldest demographic structure of
       household heads in the sample.
 ❖     Lowest: Bach Dang (57.4 years) – An area with household heads younger than the general
       average.

4.3.4. Age of surveyed household members (excluding household heads)
91.     The total number of surveyed households is 463 (based on household head data). The total
number of recorded household members is 1,660 people. The average age of all household members
is 31.7 years; meanwhile, the average age of household heads is significantly high, reaching 62.0
years. The 30-year gap between the overall average age and the age of household heads indicates that
the multi-generational family model ('Three generations under one roof') remains very prevalent. The
elderly hold the main decision-making role (household heads), while the younger generation lives
together to provide care or remains dependent.
92.     Among all household members, females have a significantly higher average age than males
(Female: 35.3 years vs. Male: 27.4 years), suggesting that women's life expectancy is higher than
that of men in the project implementation area. Detailed information on the age distribution of
household members is presented in the table below.
                     Table 9. Age Distribution of Surveyed Household Members
 Variable                                 Obs           Mean         Std. Dev.            Min          Max

 Household members                              1,66    31.72                     20.06          0           95

 Male members                                   754     27.41                     18.10          0           92

 Female members                                 906     35.29                     20.89          0           95

                                   Source: Socio-Economic Survey, December 2025 in October 2025

4.3.6. Educational background of household heads
94.     The survey results indicate that respondents with lower secondary education account for the
largest proportion at 43.20%, concentrated mainly in Nam Trieu, Bach Dang, and Hoa Binh wards.
This followed by those who have completed upper secondary school at 25.92% concentrated in An
Duong and Bach Dang. The rates for primary education and university/post-graduate education are
15.55% (concentrated in Nam Trieu and Hoa Binh) and 6.48%, respectively. Additionally, illiterate
household heads account for only 2.16% primarily found in Bach Dang and Nam Trieu. The
education level of the household head also influences the economic conditions, division of labor, and
other information within the household. Detailed information on the education level of household

4.3.8. Occupations of household heads
96.     The survey results indicate that among the 463 interviewed households, household heads
engaged in agricultural production account for the highest proportion at 46%. This is followed by
homemakers and retirees, ranking second at 35.0% – a figure that accurately reflects the age statistics
of household heads mentioned above. Next are freelance laborers (hired labor, casual work, etc.) at
12.31%, trading and services at 10.8%, fishing at 5.1%, and those with loss of working capacity at
6.05%. Additionally, other occupations account for small proportions, such as livestock breeding,
seafood processing, small-scale trading, retirement, housekeeping, or working for private companies.
Details on the occupations of household heads in each commune are presented in the table below.

4.3.12. Household’s domestic water sources
100. Clean water is one of the criteria used to evaluate the development level and civilization of
urban residents. In the survey on water sources, the majority of surveyed households are using tap
water, with 455 households, accounting for 98.27%. There are 4 households (0.86%) stating they still
use drilled/dug well water, and 1 household (0.22%) still uses water from the irrigation system for
domestic purposes.

101. Relying on a single water source can pose high risks if that source is interrupted or encounters
an incident, leading to difficulties in daily activities. However, along with the increasing speed of
urbanization, the issue of water security, supply, and assurance of clean water is always a top priority;
therefore, the trend of using a single water source in urban areas is increasing.
102. Regarding production activities, most respondents stated that they use water from the clean
water supply system with 279 households (60.26%); 76 households (16.41%) use water from rivers
and canals for production; and 67 households (14.47%) use rainwater for production activities.

113. The above data in the area shows a clear disparity, meaning that most households have income
at average, while a few households have high income.The median income reaches 4.25 million VND
per person, lower than the average of 5.36 million VND per person, indicating significant disparity
between households. The median being lower than the average shows that most households have
income below the average level. According to urban near-poor household standards, the median
indicates that most households exceed the urban poverty standard.
114. Income Sources: Many affected households have more than one income source. Households
with salary income account for a high proportion of 329/463 households. Next are income from
agricultural production activities and trading/services. Among households with agricultural income
sources, most have additional income sources. SES survey results show that among 243 households
with agricultural income, 225 households have income from other activities such as salaries,
trading/business/services, and other sources.

83.59% of households reporting monthly expenditures for such purposes. Other specific expenditures
account for low proportions, such as house repair/construction (12.64% of households),
production/business costs (25.72% of households), and other expenses (0.67% of households).
4.3.16. Household borrowing needs
116. Borrowing capital from credit institutions plays an important role in supporting households
to achieve production and business goals, especially in the fields of agriculture and aquaculture.
Access to credit provides households with financial resources to purchase materials, expand
production scale, invest in infrastructure, or apply new technologies to improve productivity. The
survey results show that only 9.29% of surveyed households have borrowed capital from credit
institutions. Among them, 30.23% of borrowers used the funds for investment in trading/services,
27.91% borrowed for construction and house repair, 18.6% invested in education, and notably, up to
27.91% of respondents stated that the purpose of the loan was for medical examination and treatment.
Regarding loan sources, households mainly borrowed from relatives, friends, and neighbors,
accounting for 46.51%; other channels such as the Vietnam Bank for Agriculture and Rural
Development (Agribank) and the Vietnam Bank for Social Policies (VBSP) accounted for 16.28%
and 11.63%, respectively; borrowing from other banks accounted for 25.58%, and borrowing from
moneylenders accounted for only 2.33%.

4.3.19. Use of electricity and fuel
119.      Survey results show that all affected households now have access to the national grid.
120. Cooking fuels are diverse, with two main sources being electricity and gas. Only a very small
fraction (2 households) uses firewood or straw as cooking fuel. Specifically, 367 households use gas,
and 93 households use electricity for cooking activities.
4.3.20. Legal Status of Land
121. For households, land and housing are not only places of residence but also valuable assets;
therefore, the Land Use Rights Certificate serves as the legal basis to prove the legality of citizens'
land use. For affected households, the Land Use Rights Certificate is an important basis and legal
foundation for determining compensation value as well as support amounts for the households.

4.3.21. Gender issues
126. In order to understand the division of labor by gender, it has been conducted interview with
households by questions related to who will be mainly responsible for tasks such as shopping,
cooking, and taking care of children.… in the family. It will consider different duties and
responsibilities between men and women, who will do, what will they do, when will they do, how
and how long etc. and social recognition of labor. The division of tasks on the basis of gender is
known and understood by all members of each community/society.
127. Women in the family not only have to be responsible for production work, but also carry the
burden of jobs that are considered as " odd jobs", making them always "busy". The progressing of a
community is measured by the progressing of women in that community. And the progressing of
women is reflected in whether they are "liberated", shared housework in the family and participated
in the common work of society? In general, women must fully perform the following tasks: (i)
Production work (production of goods and services for household consumption or income generation)
(ii) Reproduction work (giving birth, taking care of children, doing housework and maintaining

                                                                                                                     P a g e | 38
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

family life) (iii) Community work (providing and maintaining resources used by people such as
water, health care, education, leadership participation). Men tend to participate in community and
production work.
128. Regarding the division of labor in the region, there is no difference compared to other studies
and gender-based division of labor analyses in Vietnam: women participate in production,
reproduction, and childcare/family care activities, while men mainly participate in production tasks
and attend meetings at the commune and residential group levels.

4.3.22. Vulnerable households
131. Out of the total 463 households affected by the project, 44 households belong to vulnerable
groups, comprising: 14 female-headed households with dependents; 07 elderly households (small
size of 1-2 people) living alone or without support; and 23 policy households with meritorious
services to the revolution.

5.       DESIRES AND CAPACITY FOR PARTICIPATION

5.1.     Priority Areas for Local Development
132. Based on the results of community consultations in the wards/communes within the project
area and discussions with local authorities, both residents and local authorities have identified the
following priority investment areas to improve the quality of life:
     •   Improvement of drainage and environmental sanitation systems: This is the top priority
         (receiving the highest consensus rate). Residents in inner-city wards (such as Le Chan and
         Ngo Quyen) expressed a strong desire for the Project to comprehensively address localized
         flooding during the rainy season and high tides, as well as to upgrade open sewer systems that
         currently cause pollution. Additionally, improving the water quality of the Re River to ensure
         the quality of domestic water supply is a key concern.
     •   Upgrading of transport infrastructure and alleys: The development of public transport
         infrastructure will facilitate convenient mobility, unlock potential for urban and industrial
         development, and progressively improve residents' living standards. Residents in densely
         populated areas specifically requested the expansion and surface upgrading of alleys and
         lanes, along with the installation of public lighting systems to ensure public security and
         traffic safety.
     •   Public welfare facilities: Construction and upgrading of cultural houses (community
         centers), playgrounds for children, and green spaces, which are currently severely lacking in
         high-density central wards.
5.2.     Desires to Contribute Opinions and Monitor Project Implementation
133. The community expressed their capacity and willingness to participate in various stages of
the Project through the following mechanisms:
     •   Participation in the Community Supervision Board (CSB): Representatives from
         residential groups expressed a desire to join the CSB to directly monitor construction
         progress, material quality, and contractors' compliance with occupational safety and
         environmental sanitation regulations.
     •   Participation in the Inventory of Losses (IOL) and Compensation process: Residents
         requested direct involvement in the measurement, asset inventory, and determination of land
         origin to ensure accuracy, transparency, and fairness.
     •   Feedback Mechanism: The community desires the establishment of rapid feedback channels
         (such as hotlines, community Zalo groups, and suggestion boxes at cultural houses) to timely
         report issues arising during construction (e.g., noise, dust, structural cracking of houses, etc.).
5.3.     Desires for Project Information
134. To ensure social consensus and effective monitoring, residents have expressed a need to
access the following information:
     •   Information on Compensation and Resettlement Policies: This is the most urgent need.
         Residents wish to know details regarding compensation unit prices (for land, structures, and
         crops), livelihood transition support policies, and resettlement arrangement plans (location
         and infrastructure) prior to the implementation of land acquisition.
     •   Construction Plan and Schedule: Residents need to be clearly informed of the
         commencement date, completion date, and traffic diversion plans to proactively arrange their
         daily lives and business activities, thereby minimizing disruptions to their living conditions.

                                                                                                P a g e | 41
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

      •   Environmental Impacts and Mitigation Measures: Information regarding potential
          impacts (dust, noise, vibration) and the contractors' commitments to implementing mitigation
          measures.
      •   Diverse Information Access Channels: In addition to traditional methods (public
          loudspeakers, posting at the Ward People's Committee headquarters, residential group
          meetings), residents proposed that the Project provide information via digital platforms such
          as the Ward's electronic information portal and residential group Zalo groups (aligning with
          the strong digital transformation trends in wards like An Bien and Hong Bang).
6.        PROJECT IMPACT ASSESSMENT
6.1.      Positive Impacts
135. Upon implementation, the Project is expected to deliver the following positive socio-
economic impacts:
  ❖       General Economic Benefits: Economic activities in Hai Phong City, such as trade, services,
          and tourism, will benefit significantly from the Project through improved transport
          infrastructure, enhanced inter-regional connectivity, and better environmental sanitation
          conditions.
  ❖       Urban Development & Connectivity: The construction of Ring Road 3 creates a prerequisite
          for a breakthrough in Hai Phong City’s urban spatial development towards the East,
          enhancing connectivity with surrounding provinces such as Hanoi, Hai Duong, Bac Ninh,
          Quang Ninh, and Thai Binh. This, in turn, creates a driving force for socio-economic
          development.
  ❖       Infrastructure & Environment: Investments in renovating and upgrading the drainage system,
          constructing regulator gates on the Tam Bac River, and rehabilitating the Re River basin will
          comprehensively improve drainage capacity, minimize inundation, and enhance the City’s
          climate change resilience. Additionally, the investment in the wastewater collection and
          treatment system (XLNT) will improve the City's environmental conditions, enhance the
          quality of life and public health, support climate adaptation, and create impetus for the City's
          development.
  ❖       Impact of Component 2: Upon completion of Component 2 investments, it is estimated that
          approximately 1,630 ha out of the total 4,700 ha within the project scope will see direct
          improvements in flood conditions. This corresponds to approximately 40,000 direct
          beneficiaries out of a total of 88,000 direct and indirect beneficiaries. The number of
          beneficiaries from the improvement of Re River water quality is over one million people,
          accounting for more than half of Hai Phong's urban population, thereby ensuring water
          security.
  ❖       Impact of Component 3: Following the investment in Component 3, the Project is expected
          to reduce flooding for the entire historical center of Hai Phong, covering a catchment area of
          approximately 300 ha. This corresponds to the entire population of this area, with 26,000
          direct beneficiaries from flood resolution out of a total of nearly 32,000 beneficiaries.
  ❖       Capacity Building & Awareness: Furthermore, the Project implementation will enhance
          community awareness regarding health and environmental sanitation and strengthen the
          sustainability of urban infrastructure development services through technical assistance
          activities for climate-resilient urban management.

6.2.    Impacts on Land Acquisition, Livelihood and Resettlement
136. Among four components of the project, component 1 and componet 2 requires land
acquisition for construction of roads, river embankment. Project impact on land acquisition is
substantial. An initial inventory of losses (IOL) was conducted by the PMU and social consultants
based on basic design of the road and the river to collect data and information for the preparation of
the RP. Therefore, the resettlement data provided by this RP is not the final data to be used for
compensation. Results of the IOL are summarized as follows.
➢       Impacts on households and organizations
137. It is estimated that approximately 2,460 households and organizations will be affected.
Among them, 2,433 households, equivalent to 9,245 people, will be permanently affected by the
project due to land recovery. Of these, 1,292 households (equivalent to 53.14%) will be severely
affected in their livelihoods/income (due to losing more than 30% of their total agricultural land area),
350 households affected by housing and architectural structures, of which 181 households will need
to relocate. Among 181 households to be relocated, there are 99 household qualify to family split.
Therefore, the project will need to arrange 280 resettlement plots for: (i) 181 households whose
houses and residential land are affected and have to be relocated, and (ii) 99 households living
together and are entitled to household split. There are no affected households belonging to ethnic
minority group, and there are no ethnic minorities in the project area.


                                                                                                P a g e | 44
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

138. According to the Inventory of Losses (IOL) survey conducted between September and
December 2025, a total of 350 households will be impacted by the loss of their houses and residential
land. Of these, 181 households will lose both their houses and residential land entirely and do not
have no other shelter to live within their wards. These households are therefore eligible for
resettlement arrangements. Additionally, 99 households currently living with the relocating families
meet the criteria for household separation and will be allocated at least one minimum-sized
resettlement plot. In total, 280 households will be entitled to relocate to the two designated
resettlement areas. The remaining 169 households, whose entire houses will also be affected, will be
permitted to rebuild on their remaining land parcels, as these parcels meet the city’s residential land
threshold of 60 m².

144. According to the IOL survey results, there are 51 households affected in business activities

➢         Impact on vulnerable households and ethnic minorities
145. According to the inventory results, there are 175 households belonging to vulnerable groups,
and no ethnic minority households reside in the project area. Among these, there are 81 policy
households, 10 poor households, 49 female-headed households with dependents, and 35 households
with disabled members, elderly living alone without support.
➢         Public assets and cultural heritages
146. According to the damage inventory results, the land recovery area of the project partially
affects one pagoda, one worship shrine, and one communal house. During the design process, the
design consultant and the Project Management Board worked with the local authorities to avoid
relocating these structures.
6.3.      Negative Impacts of Involuntary Resettlement
147. Involuntary resettlement involves unavoidable compulsory displacement during project
implementation, affecting affected persons (APs) who must rebuild housing, restore assets, and
recover incomes. APs include those facing compulsory land acquisition by World Bank-funded
projects, resulting in displacement or loss of residence; loss of assets or property use rights; loss of
income sources or livelihoods regardless of relocation; and restricted access to legally designated or
conservation areas impacting APs' livelihoods.
148.      Risks from involuntary resettlement include:
       (i) Lack of employment and income for some land-losing farmers
  ❖       Unemployment and income shortages among land-losing farmers rank among top concerns
          in the project area, where most residents depend on agriculture with high seasonality, low
          skills, and limited education hindering non-farm job access. Residents seek new job
          opportunities and training readiness, recognizing stable non-farm income potential with
          suitable skills.
  ❖       Low-skilled laborers face rising unemployment as an urgent social issue due to low education
          failing job transition training, lacking business ideas, avoiding export labor markets. Most
          low-skilled workers belong to poor agricultural households in economic hardship,
          necessitating multifaceted education support during agricultural land restoration to meet labor
          market demands
  ❖       Vulnerable groups at higher poverty risk from resettlement include: (i) female-headed
          households (absent/deceased/disabled husbands with dependents); (ii) disabled persons
                                                                                              P a g e | 50
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

          (work-incapable), unsupported elderly; (iii) poor per Ministry of Labor-Invalids and Social
          Affairs criteria; (iv) landless persons; (v) ethnic minorities. Compensation and restoration
          policies should include exceptions for vulnerable needing extra time before
          livelihood/relocation impacts, plus economic restoration support, training, and other aid—
          especially for income losers—to enhance resilience and future incomes
       (ii) Grave relocation
149. Surveys reveal scattered informal graves in dense residential areas (e.g., 2 graves on affected
residential land from Road No. 4 construction in Residential Area 22, Vinh Hai Ward). Technical
options minimize relocation impacts; unavoidable cases provide relocation land and compensation
for excavation, transport, relocation, reconstruction, and direct reasonable costs per project
Resettlement Plan and project Resettlement Policy Framework.
       (iii) Poverty
150. Project areas risk re-poverty from external shocks, production disruptions, and productive
land loss during construction, severely impacting livelihoods. Local consultations highlight
livelihoods as key poverty/income drivers, making income restoration essential in social assessments
or resettlement plans.
       (iv) Access restrictions
151. Investments cross agricultural/residential lands, disrupting farm /workplace /public facility
/utility access and daily life/social networks.
       (v) Gender impacts
  ❖       Most residents recognize positive project effects and support it, but land
          acquisition/resettlement inevitably significantly affects lives—especially female-headed
          households. Project compensation/resettlement policies prioritize female-headed households
          with mitigation ensuring stability.
  ❖       Implementation emphasizes women/female-headed households as beneficiaries with equal
          participation for sustainability, addressing vulnerables (landless poor, female-headed,
          disabled, children, unsupported elderly) via tailored designs.
152. Given land acquisition, employment/livelihood impacts, a Resettlement Plan (RP) must cover
the entire project, complying with World Bank involuntary resettlement policies and Vietnamese law
to minimize unwanted effects on affected households.
6.4.      Impacts on Community Health and Safety
153. During construction, increased builders and spontaneous migrant workers strain local health
stations during outbreaks. Labor accidents may arise from safety non-compliance (e.g., spilled
materials, unsafe transport, speeding/weaving vehicles), mainly affecting locals unfamiliar with
procedures, overworking, or machinery mishaps.
154. Construction-induced solid waste/wastewater from services, household livestock,
workers/tourists creates dirty puddles/stagnant water, contaminating sources/air, breeding
flies/mosquitoes, risking diarrhea, dengue, malaria without prevention/treatment/hygiene.
6.5.      Emerging Social Evils
155. Many opinions express concern that the influx of non-local laborers during construction may
disrupt project area security and order, causing social instability. When construction workers reside
in the area, they interact with locals; their movements may attract traders—including prostitution,
viewed by residents as "social evils" like prostitution, drug addiction, theft—since most workers are

                                                                                           P a g e | 51
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

men living away from family. Project area residents desire strict worker management to prevent
resident conflicts.
6.6.   HIV/AIDS, STIs, and Trafficking
156. Project impacts raise HIV/AIDS infection risks during/after construction. Increased
HIV/AIDS and other sexually transmitted infections—especially via prostitution—often accompany
large-scale construction with many temporary workers.
157. For this project, most rural-origin workers require camps, posing risks like human trafficking
and drug use.
6.7.   Temporary Impacts
158.   Temporary economic activities in the project area will suffer, including:
  ❖    Construction disrupting local economics, especially roads, culverts, Cai River dikes hindering
       production:
  ❖    Traffic rerouting or lane reductions;
  ❖    Restricted shop/restaurant/business sales along roads due to land acquisition;
  ❖    Hindered external resources entering project area.
  ❖    Obstructed product transport out for trade;
  ❖    Indirect negative economic effects from direct construction impacts.
159.   Dust and noise pollution expected during construction/operation will affect locals.
160. Non-displaced households losing partial land may find remnants fragmented/hard to access
via new roads. Construction blocking roads/irrigation disrupts crop cycles, pond aquaculture
management, harvest logistics, reducing yields or causing temporary income loss.
161. Detailed ESIA facilities like Trung Ha Kindergarten, Le Lai Service Company, logistics sites,
temples face partial land loss/temporary access limits, impacting teaching schedules, community
events, business, worship access—causing operational difficulties/community concerns despite
temporariness.
6.7.1. Impacts on Re River Fishing
162. Before Cai Tack sluice construction, Re River teemed with aquatic resources as household
livelihoods, connected to sea allowing tidal marine fish into brackish/freshwater. However, since
~1980s sluice for river regulation, fish stocks plummeted, quality declined, shrimp/fish sizes shrank;
now only ~20 small boats fish, no longer main income.
163. The results of in-depth interviews with Mr. T., a fisherman operating a boat on Re River,
indicate that fishing currently plays a supplementary role in the household’s livelihood. Mr. T.
reported that fishing is carried out mainly during spare time, when there is no other primary work.
At present, the fishery resources on Re River consist predominantly of small-sized tilapia; only
occasionally are a few fish weighing over 1 kg caught. Larger fish are usually sold to generate
additional income, while smaller fish are consumed by the household and shared with relatives and
acquaintances. On average, Mr. T. goes out fishing by boat about once or twice per week, suggesting
that current fishery exploitation on Re River is small-scale and no longer serves as a main source of
income. In recent years, his fishing activities have continued normally, even during the recent period
when An Hai Company constructed the revetment along the river.
164. Also serving domestic/irrigation water, Re River operator maintains regularly; currently
building ~200m upstream embankment. Wide riverbed (50-60m segments), embankment occupies
~10m, minimally impacting navigation/fishing. Phased construction minimally affects small local
                                                                                           P a g e | 52
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Hai Phong fishing; An Duong Ward officials confirm no flow blocks/navigation issues during
operations/maintenance/embankment.
6.7.2. Impacts on Component 3 Affected Households' Temporary Business
165. Construction disrupts roadside business households along culvert line. HDPE D315-D500
collector line spans 2,600m; total construction ~65 days (2 fronts).
166. Night construction/day restoration uses segmented 20-30m rolling method, piping/terrain
restored sequentially. Dense/narrow roads: night work/day cover (2cm steel plates). Wide/low-
traffic: daytime.
167. Preliminary survey of temporarily affected Component 3 businesses includes: (1)
eateries/cafes/pharmacies/lodging; (2) groceries/household goods; (3) beauty/fashion services; (4)
consulting/banking.
168. The Consultant conducted in-depth interviews with several business households operating
along the route expected to be affected. One household selling steamed rice rolls (bánh cuốn) in the
morning and evening reported that last year, when the road in front was excavated for water supply
pipe installation, the family had to temporarily suspend sales during the excavation days due to dust
and difficult access, which failed to attract customers. After the first few days, business activities
gradually returned to normal. However, throughout the repair period, the family had to close for about
ten days, resulting in significant income loss.
169. Another household selling flowers and coffee on the same route stated that flower sales were
not significantly affected during the previous excavation, due to the shop's location and the nature of
the goods. For the coffee business, since it is operated indoors, customers could still enter the
premises; therefore, the impact on revenue was minimal.
170. Drawing from the temporary impacts on business livelihoods observed in previous projects
with similar construction methods (road excavation for pipe laying), it can be concluded that applying
the segmented construction method will contribute to significantly minimizing the disruption to
business activities. However, to ensure effectiveness, it is necessary to strictly apply and comply with
measures for temporary road surface restoration and backfilling of excavated areas to maintain
customer access to business locations in the affected area.
171. The results of in-depth interviews with business households on the route previously affected
by similar impacts in past projects show that with the proposed construction plan (rolling construction
in short sections, rapid restoration), impacts on business activities can be minimized. On that basis,
the preliminary assessment indicates that it is likely no household will be affected for a duration
exceeding half a month (cumulative time of non-continuous full closure).

6.8.   The Preliminary Information on Two Resettlement Areas
Nam Trieu Resettlement Area:
  ❖    Type and grade of construction: Group-B project, technical infrastructure works, grade-III.
  ❖    Funding source: City budget.
  ❖    Investment objectives: The project aims to create a resettlement land fund to serve site
       clearance for the Hai Phong City Development Project adapting to climate change and other
       projects; to ensure the stability of life for people whose land is recovered; and to contribute
       to the completion of the city’s planning and socio-economic development.
  ❖    Project implementation period: 2025–2028.
  ❖    Compensation, support, and resettlement plan: The People’s Committee of Nam Trieu Ward
       shall organize the implementation of compensation, support, and resettlement in accordance
       with the provisions of the land law and other relevant legal regulations.
  ❖    Land use area: Approximately 13.66 hectares, consisting of two land lots (Lot No. 01: 5.10
       ha with 282 plots, with plot areas ranging from 55.0 m² to 112.1 m²/plot, maximum building
       height of 7 floors, and construction density of 100%; Lot No. 02: 8.56 ha with 451 plots, areas
       ranging from 55 m² to 130.3 m², maximum height of 7 floors, and construction density of
       100%).
  ❖    Construction items: Complete construction of technical infrastructure including ground
       leveling, roads and traffic works, green areas, wastewater drainage system and wastewater
       treatment plant, water supply system, fire prevention and fighting works, electricity supply,
       lighting, parking areas, telecommunications system, and rubble stone revetments.
  ❖    Total investment capital: The total investment capital is VND 356,314,000,000.
Bach Dang Resettlement Area:
  ❖    Type and grade of construction: Group-B project, technical infrastructure works, grade-III;
       the minimum service life of major works is not less than 50 years.
  ❖    Funding source: City budget.
  ❖    Investment objectives: To create a resettlement land fund serving site clearance for the City
       Development Project adapting to climate change and other projects; to stabilize the livelihood
       of affected residents; and to contribute to socio-economic development.
  ❖    Project implementation period: 2025–2028.




                                                                                                   P a g e | 55
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

  ❖    Compensation, support, and resettlement plan: The People’s Committee of Bach Dang Ward
       shall organize the implementation of compensation, support, and resettlement in accordance
       with the provisions of the land law and other relevant legal regulations.
  ❖    Complete construction of technical infrastructure on an area of approximately 19.94 hectares.
       Items include ground leveling, roads and traffic works, green areas, wastewater drainage
       system and wastewater treatment plant, water supply system, fire prevention and fighting
       works, electricity supply, lighting, telecommunications system, rubble stone revetments.
  ❖    Total investment capital: VND 544,098,000,000.

7.      SOCIAL         ACCOUNTABILITY,                 AND       MONITORING              PUBLIC
        CONSULATION AND INFORMATION DISCLOSURE

7.1.    Information Disclosure, Social Accountability, and Monitoring Public Consultation
❖      Implementation method
173. Methods of information dissemination and public consultation include: rapid assessment with
participation in the rural and stakeholder consultation, Survey of affected areas, interviews with
affected households, community meetings, focus group discussions, and socio-economic interviews
and surveys.
❖      Information dissemination and consultation contents
174. In the early stages of project preparation, local authorities at all levels were informed about
the project, its objectives, components and activities, and were consulted and engaged in discussions
about local development needs and priorities. Local authorities were consulted on the potential
negative impacts of the project, measures to minimize the impacts and the benefits of the project.
Local authorities were also consulted on their consensus and commitment to implement the
resettlement policy as described in the RP. After the project is approved, the provincial and local
mass media will widely introduce the project in public places, including the objectives, components
and activities of the project.
175. During the preparation of the HPRSDP Project, public consultation was extensively
conducted through direct consultation meetings, household surveys, focus group discussions, in-
depth interviews, and written consultations with local authorities and relevant agencies.
  ❖     Direct community consultation meetings: A total of 16 consultation meetings were
        organized in 11 out of 13 wards within the Project area (meetings were not held in Thuy
        Nguyen and Ngo Quyen wards due to the absence of residents within the affected scope),
        with 1,917 participants (1,145 Males and 772 Females). The collected opinions have been
        acknowledged and integrated into the ESIA, RP, and the project's environmental and social
        management plans.
  ❖     Socio-Economic Survey, December 2025 (SES): Conducted with a sample size of 463
        households across 08 wards via quantitative survey. Additionally, an Inventory of Losses
        (IOL) was conducted using 2,652 forms for households affected by land acquisition for the
        Project.
  ❖     Focus Group Discussions (FGDs): 11 FGDs were conducted in 11 wards, focusing on
        livelihoods, social impacts, gender, and vulnerable groups.
  ❖     In-depth Interviews (KIIs): 11 in-depth interviews were conducted. Target groups included:
        severely affected households, vulnerable households, representatives of local authorities,
        business households along the drainage and wastewater collection routes, and fishing
        households on the Re River.
  ❖     Written consultation: The ESIA/RP documents were sent to: (i) 12 affected agencies/units,
        and (ii) 13 Ward People’s Committees and Fatherland Front Committees. The results of the
        written consultation are presented in Table 5 and Table 6.
  ❖     Online consultation: The Project Owner sent Document No. 1593/BQL-DA1 dated 01
        October 2025 to the Ministry of Agriculture and Environment (MAE) to conduct consultation
        by disclosing information on the Electronic Information Portal for a duration of 15 days, in
        accordance with regulations.

                                                                                          P a g e | 58
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

176. According to the Project's Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP), based on the roles,
responsibilities, and potential levels of interest of key stakeholders identified through initial
consultation activities, Table 4 summarizes and estimates the levels of interest, impact, and influence
of each stakeholder group, in which:
 ❖      "Impact" refers to the Project's impacts (including both positive and negative) on each key
        stakeholder group across Project phases;
 ❖      "Influence" refers to the degree of influence stakeholders have on the Project implementation
        process;
 ❖      The levels of "impact" and "influence" are assessed across three categories: High, Medium,
        and Low.
177. It should be noted that these levels of interest, impact, and influence may evolve throughout
the Project implementation. However, the current profile of stakeholder interest, impact, and
influence, as presented in Table 44, serves as a crucial basis for determining appropriate and effective
engagement strategies for each stakeholder group, ensuring their meaningful contribution to the
process and outcomes regarding the Project's environmental and social aspects.
