7.2.     Information Disclosure
179. To ensure participation from affected communities, households, local authorities, and
related organizations in sharing project information, consulting technical solutions, predicting
land/income/on-land asset impacts, etc., information disclosure significantly advances project
preparation/implementation with community/authority/Project Management Board consensus.
This minimizes conflict/other risks while enhancing investment efficiency and social significance.
180. The World Bank's ESS10 states that the Vietnamese version of this resettlement plan will
be available for public viewing at the Hai Phong Project Management Unit for Regional and
Sustainable Development Projects (HPRSDP PMU) offices, the People's Committees of
communes, and the electronic information portal of Hai Phong city. The English version will be
uploaded on the World Bank website before and after this plan is approved.
181. In addition, project information booklets (PIBs) will be prepared and distributed to affected
parties during the project preparation and implementation phases to ensure that affected people
are clearly aware of the project and its benefits. The PIBs will include information about the
compensation and support policies mentioned in the resettlement plan.
            Table 46. Information Disclosure, Social Accountability and Monitoring
  INFORMATION DISCLOSURE, SOCIAL ACCOUNTABILITY, AND MONITORING.
 1. Consultation and Information Disclosure
 The primary objective of information disclosure and public consultation is to ensure community,
 household, local authority, and relevant organization participation in sharing project information,
 technical option selection, and anticipated impacts on land, income, and land-based assets.
 Information disclosure significantly contributes to project progress during preparation,
 implementation, and operation with community and Project Management Board consent,
 minimizing conflict risks, enhancing investment efficiency, and social significance. The
 information disclosure and public consultation program must ensure:
       - Local authorities and affected local resident representatives participate in project
         preparation and decision-making processes. The Project Management Board coordinates
         closely with communes/wards during implementation. Affected local resident
         participation continues by requiring communes/districts to invite their representatives to
         compensation, site clearance, and resettlement committees and partial resettlement
         activities.
       - Sharing all project items and activities as planned with affected persons.
       - Gathering information on affected persons' needs and priorities, along with their feedback
         on proposed policies and activities.
       - Ensuring affected persons receive full notification of decisions directly impacting their
         income and livelihoods, with opportunities for social participation and decision-making
         on directly affecting issues.
       - Maintaining transparency in all land acquisition, compensation, resettlement, and
         restoration activities
 For the World Bank, affected persons require full information and consultation on resettlement and
 compensation options. Consultation serves as the starting point for all resettlement-related
 activities. Resettlement-affected individuals may worry about livelihood and community relation
 disruptions or unassured rights. Participation in resettlement planning and management alleviates

                                                                                        P a g e | 66
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

 fears and involves them in directly affecting life decisions. Non-consultative resettlement
 implementation risks unsuitable, ineffective strategies. Without consultation, affected persons may
 react negatively, causing social difficulties, delaying objectives, increasing costs, or project
 abandonment. Thus, consultation transforms initial opposition into constructive engagement.
 In Vietnam, enhancing grassroots democracy follows Central Committee Directive No. 30-CT/TW
 on "Building and Implementing Grassroots Democracy," Government Decree 79/2003/ND-CP on
 commune/ward/town democracy regulations, and the current Ordinance on Grassroots Democracy
 Implementation. Key slogan: "people know, people discuss, people do, people monitor." Ordinance
 34/2007/PL-UBTVQH11 specifies local authority-community consultation issues before
 decisions, including project-related compensation, support, and resettlement plans in
 wards/communes. Land Law 2013 Article 67(4) mandates disclosing compensation, support, and
 resettlement plans to affected persons.
 Thus, consultation and participation innovate Vietnamese project implementation. This policy
 addresses gaps in local authorities, residents, and inexperienced implementers. To encourage
 stakeholder consultation participation:
       - Identify and engage all stakeholders, especially project area residents and affected persons
         (men, women, poor, ethnic minorities) in public meetings.
       - Develop participation strategies for project planning, implementation, monitoring, and
         evaluation.
       - Create content and targets for promotion strategies, information disclosure, and
         negotiation processes for affected persons' rights and benefits.
       - Involve stakeholders in decision-making across implementation (e.g., design options,
         compensation methods, affected person consultations, compensation modalities,
         timelines). Establish schedules for activities like information campaigns, compensation
         levels/forms, welfare, relocation sites, and plans.
       - Build grievance redress procedures
 Public consultation occurs regularly with detailed design units for project items, ensuring
 community-involved proposals that limit negative community impacts, fostering community-
 friendly projects. Consultation also involves management/operation units for design input.
 During construction, project owners announce via mass media on construction activities, timelines,
 affected resident support measures, and community feedback processes. Affected persons (APs)
 receive project policy/procedure notifications to maintain post-project livelihoods. APs can direct
 project queries to the Project Management Board for resolution.
 2. Consultation and Information Disclosure Content and Forms

8.       IDENTIFICATION OF STAKEHOLDER AND ROLE

8.1.     Overview of Stakeholders
182. The Governing Authority: People's Committee of Hai Phong City; Supervisory Agency:
Department of Finance of Hai Phong City; Investor: Project Management Board for Investment
and Construction of Transportation and Agricultural Works of Hai Phong; Implementing Agency:
Project Management Board for Investment and Construction of Transportation and Agricultural
Works of Hai Phong; Donor: World Bank (WB).


                                                                                        P a g e | 67
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

8.2.    Roles of Stakeholders in Project Management and Implementation
       Related Agencies
183. Ministry of Finance (MOF): Guides the Governing Authority in building project lists,
integrating ODA project lists, leads preparation of content, organizes mobilization and
coordination of suitable ODA sources; coordinates with relevant agencies to submit to the Prime
Minister for approval of domestic financial mechanisms for projects before signing loan
agreements. Implements verification of withdrawal documents and disbursement of IDA/IBRD
loans for projects per current regulations. Simultaneously manages finances using project funds
and accounts state budget plans for these funds, monitors international ODA agreement
negotiations with donors; monitors, supervises, and periodically evaluates investments, serves as
focal point for handling issues within authority or proposing Prime Minister resolutions.
184. Ministry of Agriculture and Environment (MAE): Guides, inspects, and coordinates
ministries in reviewing environmental issues and waste management for projects.
185. Ministry of Construction (MOC): Implements legal regulations on guiding and controlling
investment project preparation, conducts design appraisals per regulations or inspects technical
design appraisal, construction drawings, and cost estimates for components, project management
costs; guides, inspects construction quality management, inspection operations, evaluation, and
quality certification; coordinates with Ministry of Finance to guide principles and methods for
determining drainage service unit prices and inspects/supervises implementation.
186. Technical Infrastructure Department - Ministry of Construction: Plays key role supporting
project implementation, capacity building, and institutional reform. Participates in reviewing,
preparing documents, and supporting signing/monitoring service contracts between local
authorities and service providers, reviewing/supporting preparation/approval of cost norms and
drainage service unit prices, reviewing/proposing institutional reforms, public utility
corporatization, restructuring/reorganization of enterprises; reviews/proposes public-private
management models, revolving funds for household connections; supports design/implementation
of Information-Education-Communication Program.
187. State Bank of Vietnam: Leads coordination with Ministry of Finance and relevant agencies
to prepare content, negotiate, and sign international agreements with donors, acts as official
borrower representative per international agreements assigned/authorized by the President or
Government.
       People's Committee of Hai Phong City
188. People's Committee of Hai Phong City serves as Governing Authority, assigns Project
Management Board for Investment and Construction of Transportation and Agricultural Works of
Hai Phong as Investor. Builds project proposals, submits/explains to relevant ministries and
donors; coordinates with Ministry of Finance and State Bank of Vietnam to submit to Prime
Minister for signing international ODA/concessional loan agreements for the project, coordinates
loan agreement negotiations with Ministry of Finance; directs Investor to implement signed
agreements, performs state management of project ODA/concessional loan capital, approves
project documents authorized by Prime Minister; implements land acquisition, compensation, and
site clearance per approved Resettlement Policy Framework; allocates central budget capital for
foreign loan debt repayment for re-lending capital.
       Project Steering Committee and Implementing Organizations



                                                                                      P a g e | 68
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

189. Project Management Board and implementing organizations include provincial committee
leaders, Hai Phong City People's Committee representatives, and leaders of relevant
Departments/Sectors to be established. Responsible for supporting Hai Phong City People's
Committee in quickly resolving outstanding issues during project implementation to meet donor
commitments. Holds regular monthly meetings and ad-hoc meetings per provincial People's
Committee requests to resolve sector-specific issues, thereby accelerating project progress
       Project Management Board
190. Project Management Board directly manages investment projects per regulations, signed
agreements, and tasks assigned by Governing Authority; develops overall plans and detailed
annual plans for program/project implementation; conducts bidding activities and contract
management; disburses, manages project finances and assets; implements project implementation
monitoring and evaluation; inspects, accepts, and hands over project products upon completion;
completes audits, hands over project assets; prepares completion reports and project settlements.
       Ward Level
191.    Ward People's Committees have the following responsibilities:
 ❖      Notify land acquisition if authorized by provincial People's Committee.
 ❖      Approve compensation, support, and resettlement plans prepared by ward-level
        compensation/support/resettlement unit, submitted to city People's Committee for
        comments;
 ❖      Issue land acquisition decisions for individuals/households;
 ❖      Resolve complaints/denunciations from affected persons within authority.
192. City-level compensation/support/resettlement implementing unit (called City
Compensation Council) responsible for compensation and site clearance for city-area works,
including:
 ❖      Establishing ward/commune working groups and managing functions, assigning
        ward/commune staff to support district compensation council and Project Management
        Board in detailed measurements, preparing land acquisition dossiers, preparing
        Resettlement Action Plans, and implementing resettlement activities.
 ❖      Preparing and implementing regular resettlement activities within city scope.
 ❖      Responsible for inventory and land recovery, completing compensation, preparing
        compensation tables for competent authority approval, and direct payment to affected
        households after receiving funds;
 ❖      Preparing land and land procedures for resettlement for relocated households;
 ❖      Designating intermediaries to resolve public complaints on compensation policies and
        entitlements;
 ❖      Establishing (if needed) ward/commune compensation councils and directing resettlement
        implementation activities;
 ❖      Certifying affected persons' legal documents on land use/transfer to meet compensation
        plan requirements;
 ❖      Paying special attention to needs/aspirations of special groups (ethnic minorities) and
        vulnerable groups (children, elderly, female-headed/single-person households);



                                                                                        P a g e | 69
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

  ❖     Supporting units/organizations including Project Management Board in disseminating
        information, organizing community meetings, and consulting affected persons;
  ❖     Supporting units/organizations including Project Management Board in household census
        surveys, replacement cost/price surveys, detailed measurements, and other resettlement
        activities;
  ❖     Participating in all land recovery/allocation, resettlement, livelihood restoration support,
        and other social development support activities;
  ❖     Supporting affected persons in all resettlement and living standard restoration activities.
  ❖     Notifying affected persons of compensation schedules and supervising implementation;
        signing compensation documents with affected persons;
  ❖     Ensuring full grievance redress mechanism implementation for affected persons.
        Recording all complaints and archiving. Supporting/consulting affected persons and
        promptly resolving complaints.
  ❖     Closely coordinating with independent supervision consultants.
       Donor
193. World Bank conducts periodic supervision and inspections during project implementation.
Provides recommendations to remedy shortcomings for optimal project execution. Bank conducts
field inspections, reviews reports/documents, bidding, contractor selection.
8.3.    Roles of Mass Organizations and Community Organizations
194. Social mass organizations, community organizations, and project facility users play very
important roles during project implementation.
       Political-Social Organizations
195. Political-social organizations such as Fatherland Front, Women's Union, Farmers'
Association, Youth Union, Elderly Association, etc., have large memberships organized down to
hamlet/village level. Social organizations closely monitor project implementation, especially land
acquisition, compensation, and resettlement phases to ensure compliance with Bank safeguards
and current Vietnamese regulations. Mass organizations grasp issues and public feedback related
to project activities during preparation, construction, and operation. Mass organization monitoring
coordinated with community neighborhood groups plays important role helping Project
Management Board, contractors timely adjust designs/construction activities to minimize
unwanted impacts on affected residents' lives.
       Ward/Commune, Hamlet/Village Level
196. Working directly with people, reflecting emerging issues during project preparation,
construction, and operation. Hamlets/villages are final points disseminating project information to
residents and first receiving public feedback on projects. Hamlet/village cadres' roles are crucial
to ensure objectivity and timely reflection of legitimate resident aspirations/recommendations for
project implementation monitoring, minimizing unwanted life impacts. Additionally,
hamlet/village cadre opinions help build more suitable, higher economic-social efficiency project
components for directly benefiting communities.
8.4.    Coordination Among Stakeholders
197. During project preparation and implementation, stakeholder participation holds special
importance. First, participation during facility design significantly contributes to design


                                                                                         P a g e | 70
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

adjustments meeting technical requirements, increasing economic-social efficiency and
investment effectiveness, minimizing unwanted impacts from facilities on communities,
especially social/environmental impacts during construction and operation. Poor stakeholder
coordination leads to potential resource waste, project delays, negative social/environmental
impacts, and reduced investment efficiency, etc. During implementation, good stakeholder
coordination ensures on-schedule project completion.
198. One project success criterion is ensuring stakeholder participation in all project stages.
This requires developing a Participation Plan. Roles and responsibilities for achieving
participation are allocated to stakeholders as follows:

8.5.   Capacity Building Training Needs for Stakeholders
199. From practical experience implementing previous World Bank-funded projects over the
past 10 years, along with practically trained administrative apparatus, Hai Phong City People's
Committee ensures sufficient capacity to receive, organize, and successfully implement the
additional project. Simultaneously, Hai Phong City People's Committee commits to allocating
sufficient counterpart funds, human resources, and other necessary conditions for effective Coastal
Cities Sustainable Environment Project implementation. Project Management Board for
Investment and Construction of Transportation and Agricultural Works of Hai Phong has full
apparatus including Board of Directors (3 persons) with extensive project management/investment
construction experience; specialized departments with high qualifications trained in-depth by
international experts, familiar with Vietnamese Government and donor procedures; existing
experienced staff will facilitate project progress and effectiveness. Capacity building programs for
Investor via workshops, experience sharing, management procedure development will improve
project management/implementation, complying with latest donor safeguard policies
200. Additionally, Project will develop community communication programs to raise
environmental protection awareness among project-area residents. Support sector institutional
reforms to promote operation efficiency and project sustainability, while sharing experiences with
other localities on ODA project management in drainage and environmental sanitation.

9.     MITIGATION MEASURES AND INTERVENTIONS

201. Hai Phong City climate-adaptive development, per survey and public consultation results,
brings major short- and long-term benefits to residents and the city against climate impacts,
sustaining infrastructure investments and enhancing environments across city areas.While
construction and post-completion may trigger negative resident/community effects from land
acquisition/resettlement, social evils, etc., positive impacts prevail; negatives remain secondary


                                                                                                  P a g e | 72
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

and mitigable through project framework policies, donor/government regulations, guidelines, and
plans:
9.1.   Preparation and Effective Implementation of Communication Plan, Public
       Consultation, and Participation Mobilization
202. Multiple public consultations during project preparation minimize negative impact risks
and establish communication channels. Foreign loan characteristics like inconsistent
compensation/investment policies may cause complaints, so the province issues specific directives
on census, payment, compensation rates per Prime Minister-approved Resettlement Policy
Framework; Project Management Board collaborates with local authorities for broad
dissemination of investment objectives and project policies ensuring community clarity.
203. Based on communication status surveys and community wishes for project information
related to goals, a communication action plan and public consultation has been developed for
integration into project activities.
9.2.   Action Plan Preparation
204. Despite efforts to avoid land acquisition and resettlement for local residents, land and other
asset recovery needs remain unavoidable. Per World Bank requirements, the project will prepare
a Resettlement Action Plan for this project, outlining how the project minimizes affected persons'
losses and restores livelihoods through compensation, support, and/or allowances. The overall
objective of the Resettlement Action Plan is to ensure all affected persons receive replacement
value compensation for their losses and support measures to restore livelihoods—at least
equivalent to pre-project levels.
205. When asked about compensation/resettlement views, most residents desire adequate
compensation/support for stable new housing/lives, proposing market-rate compensation.
Residents also worry about project timelines, urging acceleration with quick, streamlined, fair
support/compensation/site clearance for prompt new life stabilization. Beyond complying with the
Project Resettlement Policy Framework, World Bank Involuntary Resettlement Policy ESS5, and
current Vietnamese law, these opinions require consideration/resolution in the project
Resettlement Action Plan.
206. Refer to the project Resettlement Plan, prepared at feasibility/detailed technical design
stage. This report will undergo appraisal/approval by Vietnamese side and World Bank before
project approval.
9.3.   Environmental and         Social   Impact     Assessment     Report    Preparation      and
       Implementation
207. Environmental/social impacts during project operation, noted in negative impact sections,
have been identified, assessed, and proposed mitigations in the project Environmental Impact
Assessment Report. Thus, throughout project implementation, the investor commits:
  ❖    Strictly implementing Vietnam Environmental Protection Law provisions while
       complying with World Bank environmental safety policies.
  ❖    Deploying environmental protection solutions/minimizing impacts as in Chapter 4;
       implementing environmental management program/environmental monitoring as in
       Chapter 5; fulfilling community commitments as in Chapter 6. Investor also commits to
       compensating/handling environmental pollution from incidents/risks during
       implementation; restoring environment per environmental protection law upon operation
       completion.
                                                                                        P a g e | 73
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

208. Refer to Environmental Impact Assessment Report prepared at feasibility stage. This
report will undergo appraisal/approval by Vietnamese side and World Bank before project
approval.
9.4.   Temporary Impact Mitigation Measures
209. The project owner applies technical measures to limit short-term temporary impacts from
construction activities:
9.4.1. Business Disruption Impacts
210. Technical mitigation measures to maximally reduce project effects on business operations
include:
  ❖    Segmenting construction into short sections (20–30 m)
  ❖    Using rolling method construction (complete and restore segment by segment).
  ❖    During construction, fully implement these measures:
       o     Traffic control personnel: During night shifts, deploy 2–4 controllers ensuring
             pedestrian/motorcycle safety through work areas, equipped with reflective vests,
             guiding sticks, specialized flashlights.
       o     Clear signage/guidance: Install full signage like "Slow Down – Construction Ahead,"
             "Temporary Pedestrian Path," using illumination, flashing lights, reflective tapes per
             QCVN 41:2019/BGTVT standards.
       o     Environmental measures (noise – dust – vibration): Use noise-reduced machinery or
             sound barriers. Avoid 23:00–24:00 near dense residential areas. No large concrete
             cutters during rest hours.
       o     Dust/hygiene control: Water spray before digging or vehicle entry/exit. Tarp-cover
             materials. Clean roads after each night shift.
211.   Support measures: Compensation for construction-period business disruption losses.
9.4.2. Minimizing Livelihood Impacts on Re River
212. Divide into small 20-30m segments for construction; machinery/equipment on
barges/pontoons accesses via river, minimizing site clearance and riverside resident impacts. Use
rolling method (construct and finish sequentially). Prioritize along existing embankment,
maximally avoiding current embankment demolition.
213.   For landslide-risk resident areas, drive dead piles (non-removable) before excavation.
214. Maximize hybrid embankment: Below max water level, box structure; above, sloped
embankment with grass. Embankment crest features management road plus grass-planted
sidewalk (green trees) for landscaping.
215. Dredging organic topsoil from aquaculture ponds (potentially saline mud dried on-site then
transported to permitted landfill) includes temporary on-site mud drying measures.
216. During construction, utilize embankment-adjacent land within sidewalk/grass scope for
temporary drained soil stockpiling (dry before regulated dumping).

12.    GRIEVANCE REDRESS MECHANISM (GRM)

12.1. Objectives of the GRM
229. The purpose of a Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) is to set forth procedures,
methods, and process that assist the resolution of a grievance that may arise during the Project
implementation. It offers process that is transparent, credible, and predictable to all parties that
could result in outcomes considered fair, effective, and lasting. More specifically, the GRM aims
to:
 ❖     Provide affected people with avenues for making a complaint or resolving any disputes
       that may arise during the course of Project implementation.
 ❖     Ensure that appropriate and mutually acceptable redress actions are identified and
       implemented to the satisfaction of complainants; and
 ❖     Avoid the need to resort to judicial proceedings.
12.2. Grievance Redress Mechanism
230. Grievance redress mechanism of the Project will be conducted in compliance with the Law
on Complaints (Law No.02/2011/QH13, dated 11 November 2011), and the Law on Denunciation
(Law No.25/2018/QH14, dated 12 June 2018). The grievances will be redressed in three steps
before escalating to court.
231. Affected people have the right to file complaints concerning their project-related benefits
and responsibilities, including but not limited to land acquisition, resettlement, compensation
policies, benefits, compensation rates, and other income restoration program benefits.
232. As a result, a GRM was created to address concerns about the project's land acquisition
and resettlement. This mechanism establishes procedures, methods, and processes for resolving
any complaints that may arise during project implementation. Furthermore, the mechanism
provides stakeholders with a transparent, credible, and accessible procedure that leads to fair,
effective, and long-term grievance redress. Furthermore, the availability of legal remedies, as well
as traditional and community grievance resolution methods, should be considered.
233. The GRM will be applicable to parties affected by the project, as well as parties that are
positively or negatively involved and/or may influence the project outcome.
234.   The project’s GRM includes the following steps:




                                                                                         P a g e | 80
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)



                    • People send written claims to the one-stop office of the Ward People's
                      Committee
 Ward People's      • The period of time for resolving claims is 30 days
Committee Level




                    • People send written claims to the City People's Committee
                    • The period of time for resolving claims is 45 days
  City People's
   Committee




                    • People send written claims to the People's Court at the competent level for
                      sentence
                    • The Court's decision will be final
      Court




 ❖     Step 1 – Ward People's Committee: If a household or individual has a complaint, it may
       submit a written complaint or verbally inform the WPC's one-stop office. The officer in
       charge of this office is responsible for notifying the commune leaders of a resolution. The
       WPC's will meet personally with the complainant. The time limit for settling complaints
       shall be 30 days from the time the complaints are received. The WPC's secretariat is
       responsible for documenting and filing all complaints within its jurisdiction.
 ❖     Step 2 – City People's Committee: If after 30 days, the aggrieved affected household
       does not hear from the PC of commune or if the affected household is not satisfied with
       the decision taken on his or her complaint, the affected household may bring the case,
       either in writing or in person, to the CPC. The CPC has 45 days to resolve the complaint.
       The CPC secretariat is responsible for maintaining records of complaints, actions, and
       outcomes. Complainants can also submit their complaints to the Court if they wish.
 ❖     Step 3 (final) - Administrative Court: If, after 45 days following the lodging of the
       complaint with the PPC, the aggrieved household does not hear from the CPC or if he or
       she is not satisfied with the decision taken on his or her complaint, the case may be brought
       to the Court for adjudication. The court's decision will be final.
235. The decision to resolve the complaint must be sent to the complainant and relevant parties
and posted at the committee’s office where the complaint is resolved.
236. Commune-level community organizations will incorporate existing GRMs, which are
currently chaired by commune People's Committee leaders. GRM will resolve complaints using
available local resources, addressing not only safety-related conflicts but also other issues during
the implementation process. Based on this structure, community organizations will assist in the
project's preparation, design, implementation, and future development. The GRM will be
applicable to individuals or groups directly or indirectly affected by the project, as well as those
                                                                                         P a g e | 81
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

who may have an interest in the project and/or have the potential to be positively or negatively
affected by the results of the project.
237. To minimize complaints to the province, the PMU will coordinate with the Resettlement
Committee of the communes to resolve issues. The PMU will designate an officer who will keep
a database of project-related complaints, detailing the complaint's nature, source, and date, the
complainant's name and address, the necessary actions, and the current status. In the case of a
verbal complaint, the Reception Committee will record the complaint on the Complaint Form
during the first meeting with the complainant.
238. The project's complaint resolution process, including contact information for the unit and
individual in charge of complaints, will be included in the project information leaflet and posted
at the People's Committee offices of project communes and PMU. All complaints will be recorded
and kept at the Commune People's Committee and resolved in a transparent and proactive manner
by the PMU. Documents and complaint reports will be made public. All costs related to the
complaint resolution process are paid by the project. To ensure that the GRM is consistent with
reality and is accepted by the affected people as well as the government and community, the GRM
needs to consider local traditional customs and practices to understand and resolve the complaint.
239. To avoid delays of the project implementation while ensuring compensation payments to
impacted people after their complaints have been resolved, the project will open an escrow account
for compensation and support payments at commercial banks with interest.

13.    CONCLUSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS

13.1. Conclusion
240. Hai Phong City Climate-Resilient Development Project funded by World Bank is highly
feasible. Fundamentally, multi-sector project with most operation-phase environmental-social
impacts positive, contributing to environmental sanitation improvement and city economic-social
development promotion. However, during implementation, predictable negative impacts affecting
social welfare mainly arise in site clearance preparation and construction phases:
 ❖     Site clearance phase: Largest impact disrupts residents' lives via site clearance,
       resettlement, land acquisition. This impact is relatively large, project will consume
       significant budget for resident relocation/resettlement. Currently, city reserves
       resettlement land funds, thus these impacts can be mitigated/remedied. No negative
       impacts on cultural works, historical sites, natural habitats, ecosystems.
 ❖     Construction phase: Main environmental impacts from temporary land occupation and
       social security/order. These impacts are temporary during facility construction and
       mitigable via strict construction/social management procedures.
 ❖     During World Bank pre-appraisal phase, Resettlement Plan (RP) before project
       implementation. During implementation, organize independent supervision entity/firm to
       implement Resettlement Plan. These reports meet Vietnamese Government and World
       Bank requirements on compensation/support/resettlement policies; simultaneously serve
       as documents for Investor to properly implement social welfare measures.
13.2. Recommendations
241. Ensure objectives in World Bank ESS5 on involuntary resettlement, i.e., “Restore
livelihoods and incomes of affected persons at least equal to or better than pre-project levels” via

                                                                                         P a g e | 82
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

fully preparing Resettlement Plan, building income restoration programs suitable to actual needs
of each affected group.
242. Recommend Project apply following social welfare contents during implementation and
operation:
 ❖     Communication Plan, preliminary consultation, public consultation with participation;
 ❖     Resettlement Plan (RP);
 ❖     Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) and Environmental and Social
       Management Plan (ESMP);
 ❖     Information disclosure, social accountability, and monitoring

ANNEXES

ANNEXES 1. Due Diligence Report on the Implementation of Two Resettlement Sites
        LEGAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL DUE DILIGENCE FOR
              BACH DANG RESETTLEMENT AREA – HAI PHONG CITY
          (Review of Compliance with Vietnamese Laws in Support of the HPRDP)
1. PURPOSE AND SCOPE OF THE DUE DILIGENCE
This Legal, Social and Environmental Due Diligence (DDR) has been prepared in support of the
Hai Phong City Resilience Development Project (HPRDP) to assess the compliance with
Vietnamese laws and regulations of the Investment Project for Construction of Infrastructure
of the Bach Dang Resettlement Area, which will be used to accommodate a portion of
households affected by HPRDP.
The Bach Dang Resettlement Area is an independently financed public investment project of
Hai Phong City, developed to serve multiple public investment projects, including HPRDP.
The resettlement project does not use World Bank financing and is not part of the HPRDP
investment scope.
Accordingly, this due diligence has been conducted on the basis that:
   •   The project complies with applicable Vietnamese laws and regulations on investment,
       land, environment, and resettlement;
   •   The Environmental and Social Standards (ESSs) of the World Bank ESF are not
       mandatorily applicable to the Bach Dang Resettlement Project;
   •   The review satisfies the World Bank’s requirement to demonstrate national legal
       compliance for shared resettlement areas that are not financed by the Bank.
2. OVERVIEW OF THE BACH DANG RESETTLEMENT PROJECT
2.1 General Information
   •   Project name: Investment in Construction of Infrastructure for the Bach Dang Resettlement
       Area
   •   Location: Bach Dang Ward, Hai Phong City
   •   Project owner: Hai Phong Project Management Unit for Transport and Agricultural Works
   •   Implementation period: 2025–2028 (According to the information confirmed at the
       meeting with the Project Owner, the Project is expected to be completed and put into
       operation before 30 December 2026.)
   •   Total investment: approximately VND 544.1 billion
2.2. Geographic Location
The resettlement area is developed on a total land area of approximately 19.93 hectares located in
Bach Dang Ward, Hai Phong City, as detailed below:
Location and boundaries:
   •   To the East: bordering Battalion 158 and the existing rural residential area;
   •   To the West: bordering the section from Phan Dat Canal to Gia River (adjacent to the Minh
       Duc ring road as per the approved plan);

                                                                                       P a g e | 84
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

   •   To the South: bordering Phan Dat Canal;
   •   To the North: bordering the existing rice cultivation land.




             Figure: Layout of Existing Status Bach Dang Ressettlement Area.
2.3. Land Use Scale and Technical and Social Infrastructure
The project is planned on a total land area of approximately 19.93 hectares, comprising two
resettlement areas, with the following key characteristics:
(i) Residential Resettlement Land
   •   Total number of residential resettlement plots: about 958 plots;
   •   Plot size: generally ranging from 80 m² to 120 m² per plot, in accordance with urban
       planning standards;
   •   Each plot is provided with access to internal roads and full technical infrastructure,
       including power supply, water supply, drainage, and public lighting.
(ii) Drainage and Wastewater Treatment Infrastructure
The project includes a centralized wastewater treatment plant (WWTP) with:
   •   Design capacity: The resettlement area has 1 WWTP (with designed capacity of 950
       m³/day), suitable for a planned population of about 3,832 people;
   •   Collection system: separate wastewater drainage system, independent from stormwater
       drainage;
   •   Treatment technology: biological treatment combined with sedimentation and disinfection.
(iii) Green Areas, Parks, and Public Spaces
   •   Green space and park areas: accounting for approximately 10–12% of the total project area;
   •   Including centralized parks, buffer green belts, and roadside trees along internal roads;
   •   These green spaces contribute to microclimate improvement, reduction of dust and noise,
       and provision of community spaces.
(iv) Other Social and Technical Infrastructure
   •   Internal road network and parking areas;
   •   Power supply, water supply, public lighting, and fire prevention and firefighting systems;

                                                                                        P a g e | 85
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

   •   Land reserved for small-scale public facilities serving the resettlement community;
   •   Land allocated for cultural, religious, and educational facilities.
(v) Public Utilities and Services
   •   Educational facilities: The surrounding area includes educational institutions such as
       Pham Ngu Lao High School (approximately 300 m away), Ngu Lao Primary School
       (approximately 500 m away), and Ngu Lao Kindergarten, meeting the learning needs of
       children. In addition, according to the approved planning, land within the Project area is
       allocated for the construction of a kindergarten, contributing to the completion of on-site
       educational infrastructure.
   •   Healthcare facilities: Healthcare facilities and medical service providers are located within
       a radius of approximately 0.5–1.0 km from the Project site, meeting the needs for primary
       healthcare, general medical examination and treatment, and basic first aid for resettled
       residents.
   •   Cultural and social facilities: The Project area includes a community cultural house,
       community activity points, sports grounds, and local religious and belief facilities, meeting
       the needs for cultural, spiritual activities and community interaction of local residents.
   •   Commercial and service facilities: Markets and a system of grocery stores, food services,
       pharmacies, post offices, and other essential services are located near the Project area,
       basically meeting the daily living needs of residents.




                                                                                        P a g e | 86
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)




                         Figure 1: General Layout of Bach Dang Ressettlement Area.

                                                                                     P a g e | 87
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)




   Figure 2: Location of the Bach Dang Resettlement Area and Nearby Public Utilities.
Overall, the resettlement area being located close to existing healthcare, educational, commercial,
and cultural–religious facilities enables residents to easily access essential services, quickly
stabilize their lives after resettlement, and contributes to improving quality of life, ensuring
harmonious development of the local community.
3. RELATIONSHIP WITH HPRDP
   •   The Bach Dang Resettlement Area has been planned, approved, and implemented
       independently of HPRDP;
   •   HPRDP does not provide financing for the construction of the resettlement area;
   •   The use of the Bach Dang Resettlement Area for HPRDP utilizes only part of the
       approved resettlement plots and does not result in additional construction items,
       expansion of scale, or changes to the approved design;
   •   Accordingly, the Bach Dang Resettlement Area is considered a shared resettlement site,
       prepared and implemented in compliance with Vietnam’s national legal framework.
4. APPLICABLE LEGAL FRAMEWORK
The Bach Dang Resettlement Project has been prepared and implemented in accordance with the
following key legal documents:
   •   Law on Public Investment;
   •   Land Law and its guiding decrees;
   •   Law on Environmental Protection (2020);
   •   Decree No. 08/2022/ND-CP;
   •   Decree No. 05/2025/ND-CP of the Government;
   •   Circular No. 02/2022/TT-BTNMT;
   •   Circular No. 07/2025/TT-BNNMT of the Ministry of Agriculture and Environment;
   •   National technical regulations on environment (QCVNs);


                                                                                        P a g e | 88
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

   •   Relevant regulations on planning, construction, fire prevention and firefighting, water
       supply and drainage, and waste management.
5. LEGAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL STATUS OF THE PROJECT
5.1 Investment Policy and Planning
   •   The Project has been approved in principle by the Hai Phong City People’s Committee
       (Decision No. 3639/QD-UBND dated 05 September 2025 of the Hai Phong City People’s
       Committee).
   •   The 1/500-scale detailed plan of the Bach Dang Ward Resettlement Area has been
       approved under Decision No. 814/QD-UBND dated 28 November 2025 of the Bach Dang
       Ward People’s Committee.
5.2 Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA)
   •   The Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) of the project was prepared in accordance
       with the Law on Environmental Protection (2020).
   •   The provincial-level EIA appraisal council convened the appraisal meeting in November
       2025, following receipt of the EIA dossier on 3 November 2025.
   •   Based on the appraisal comments, the project owner revised, supplemented, and submitted
       the completed EIA dossier on 4 December 2025.
   •   At the time of preparation of this DDR, the EIA dossier is under final administrative
       procedures for issuance of the approval decision by the competent authority.
From a legal and technical perspective, the EIA has completed all required steps under Vietnamese
law.
6. KEY ENVIRONMENTAL CONTENTS OF THE EIA
6.1 Environmental Baseline Conditions
   •   The current land use is mainly agricultural land (rice cultivation), interspersed with water
       bodies, existing roads, vacant land, and a number of scattered buildings and small-scale
       technical infrastructure facilities.
   •   The project area does not fall within environmentally sensitive zones and does not include
       protected areas or endangered species;
   •   Air, water, and soil quality at the time of survey complied with applicable national
       standards.
6.2 Environmental Impacts and Mitigation Measures
Pre-construction Phase

The Project will require acquisition of a total of 227,161.7 m² of land, affecting 424 households
and entities. The acquired land mainly comprises paddy land (185,081.0 m²), reflecting the
dominant land use pattern in the project area. In addition, the Project will acquire 1,372.8 m² of
residential land and 702.8 m² of residential land combined with garden land. Other affected land
includes 346.6 m² of perennial crop land, 12,864.5 m² of annual crop land, 309.6 m² of mixed
annual crop and aquaculture land, as well as 26,484.4 m² of public lands (comprising traffic land,
irrigation land), and 113.1 m² of cemetery land. There are two (02) households affected by the
acquisition of residential lands with houses on-land, will need to be on-site relocated.

To manage and mitigate potential impacts during the pre-construction phase, the Project has
allocated budget for compensation, assistance, and land clearance in accordance with current legal
regulations. Land acquisition and site clearance will be implemented following these steps:
identification of affected persons, detailed inventory; issuance of land acquisition notices; public
disclosure of Project information; preparation, consultation, and approval of compensation and
assistance plans; and payment of compensation and assistance. Households whose agricultural
land is acquired will be fully compensated in accordance with applicable unit prices and policies,
and will also be considered for assistance in livelihood stabilization, occupational transition, and
livelihood restoration. For affected irrigation canals and ditches, the Project Owner commits to
study, propose, and implement appropriate restoration/replacement solutions to ensure irrigation
capacity for agricultural production areas outside the Project boundary and drainage capacity for
adjacent residential areas; specific solutions will be calculated, documented, and publicly
disclosed during subsequent implementation stages.
Unexploded ordnance (UXO): The Project Owner will enter into a contract with a licensed military
unit to carry out UXO clearance prior to the commencement of construction works.
Construction phase:
Key impacts include dust, noise, vibration, domestic wastewater from construction workers,
construction solid waste, and localized traffic safety risks.
Mitigation measures include dust suppression, covering of materials, appropriate construction
scheduling, proper waste collection and disposal, installation of barriers and warning signs, and
implementation of occupational safety measures.
Operation phase:

                                                                                                P a g e | 90
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Main impacts include domestic wastewater, municipal solid waste, and potential odor risks from
the WWTP if not properly operated.
Mitigation measures include stable operation of the WWTP, organized solid waste collection
through municipal services, regular maintenance of technical infrastructure, and enhancement of
green spaces.
Overall environmental impacts are assessed as low to moderate and manageable.
6.3 Design Standards and Effluent Quality Requirements of the Wastewater Treatment
Plant
   •   Design capacity: 950 m³/day;
   •   Type of wastewater: domestic wastewater;
   •   Effluent quality standard: QCVN 14:2025/BTNMT – Column A;
   •   Key controlled parameters include pH, BOD₅, COD, TSS, Ammonium, Total Nitrogen,
       Total Phosphorus, and Coliform.
The application of Column A as the design effluent standard demonstrates a high level of
environmental protection, ensuring safety for the receiving water body and consistency with Hai
Phong City’s sustainable urban development objectives.
7. COMMUNITY CONSULTATION AND STAKEHOLDER PARTICIPATION
Community consultation was conducted during the EIA preparation process in accordance with
the Law on Environmental Protection (2020).
   •   Consulted stakeholders: Bach Dang Ward People’s Committee, the Fatherland Front and
       mass organizations, representatives of affected households, and local community
       members;
   •   Consultation methods: public meetings and disclosure of EIA information as required by
       law;
   •   Scale: approximately 100 participants attended the consultation meetings (based on signed
       attendance lists included in the EIA dossier).
Consultation results:
   •   The majority of opinions supported project implementation;
   •   No significant objections were recorded;
   •   Comments mainly related to implementation schedule, infrastructure quality, and
       environmental management during construction;
   •   These comments were incorporated into the EIA.
Consultation with households affected by HPRDP continues to be conducted under the
Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) and Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) of HPRDP.
8. OVERALL ASSESSMENT FOR HPRDP
The due diligence concludes that:
   •   The Bach Dang Resettlement Area has been prepared and implemented in compliance
       with Vietnamese laws and regulations;
   •   No significant unaddressed environmental or social risks have been identified;



                                                                                        P a g e | 91
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

   •   The use of this resettlement area for HPRDP does not give rise to additional legal,
       environmental, or social obligations beyond those under national law.
9. CONCLUSION
The Bach Dang Resettlement Area is an independently financed public investment project, not
using World Bank funds, and has been prepared and implemented in accordance with Vietnamese
legal requirements on investment, land, environment, and resettlement.
The resettlement area is legally and environmentally suitable for accommodating households
affected by the Hai Phong Urban Development and Flood Resilience Project (HPRDP) and
meets the World Bank’s requirement regarding compliance with the national legal framework.

        LEGAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL AND SOCIAL DUE DILIGENCE FOR
              NAM TRIEU RESETTLEMENT AREA – HAI PHONG CITY
          (Review of Compliance with Vietnamese Laws in Support of the HPRDP)
1. PURPOSE AND SCOPE OF THE DUE DILIGENCE
This Legal, Social and Environmental Due Diligence (DDR) has been prepared in support of the
Hai Phong City Resilience Development Project (HPRDP) to assess the compliance with
Vietnamese laws and regulations of the Investment Project for Construction of Infrastructure
of the Nam Trieu Resettlement Area, which will be used to accommodate a portion of
households affected by HPRDP.
The Nam Trieu Resettlement Area is an independently financed public investment project of
Hai Phong City, developed to serve multiple public investment projects, including HPRDP.
The resettlement project does not use World Bank financing and is not part of the HPRDP
investment scope.
Accordingly, this due diligence has been conducted on the basis that:
   •   The project complies with applicable Vietnamese laws and regulations on investment,
       land, environment, and resettlement;
   •   The Environmental and Social Standards (ESSs) of the World Bank ESF are not
       mandatorily applicable to the Nam Trieu Resettlement Project;
   •   The review satisfies the World Bank’s requirement to demonstrate national legal
       compliance for shared resettlement areas that are not financed by the Bank.
2. OVERVIEW OF THE NAM TRIEU RESETTLEMENT PROJECT
2.1 General Information
   •   Project name: Investment in Construction of Infrastructure for the Nam Trieu Resettlement
       Area
   •   Location: Nam Trieu Ward, Hai Phong City
   •   Project owner: Hai Phong Project Management Unit for Transport and Agricultural Works
   •   Implementation period: 2025–2028 (According to the information confirmed at the
       meeting with the Project Owner, the Project is expected to be completed and put into
       operation before 30 December 2026.)
   •   Total investment: approximately VND 356.3 billion
2.2. Geographic Location
The resettlement area is developed on a total land area of approximately 13.66 hectares located in
Nam Trieu Ward, Hai Phong City, comprising two sub-areas, as detailed below:
5.09-ha Resettlement Area
Location and boundaries:
   •   Northeast: adjacent to the Dau Cau residential area;
   •   Southwest: adjacent to the Lach Se residential area;
   •   Northwest: adjacent to the rural residential development project in the Dau Cau area;
   •   Southeast: adjacent to Lap Le Primary School area.
8.56-ha Resettlement Area

                                                                                      P a g e | 100
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Location and boundaries:
   •   Northeast: adjacent to an existing residential area;
   •   Southwest: adjacent to the cemetery area and Huong Road;
   •   Northwest: adjacent to agricultural land (paddy fields);
   •   Southeast: adjacent to the rural residential development project in the Dau Cau area.



       9-ha Resettlement Area
                                                                  Land Auction Area and
                                                                  Green Park




                 5-ha Resettlement Area




           Figure: Layout of Existing Status of Nam Triệu Ressettlement Area.
2.3. Land Use Scale and Technical and Social Infrastructure
The project is planned on a total land area of approximately 13.66 hectares, comprising two
resettlement areas, with the following key characteristics:
(i) Residential Resettlement Land
   •   Total number of residential resettlement plots: approximately 530–540 plots;
   •   Plot size: generally ranging from 80 m² to 120 m² per plot, in accordance with urban
       planning standards;
   •   Each plot is provided with access to internal roads and full technical infrastructure,
       including power supply, water supply, drainage, and public lighting.
(ii) Drainage and Wastewater Treatment Infrastructure
The project includes a centralized wastewater treatment plant (WWTP) with:
   •   Design capacity: The resettlement area has 2 WWTPs (with designed capacity os 350 and
       400 m³/day), suitable for a planned population of about 2,940 people;
   •   Collection system: separate wastewater drainage system, independent from stormwater
       drainage;
   •   Treatment technology: biological treatment combined with sedimentation and disinfection.
                                                                                      P a g e | 101
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

(iii) Green Areas, Parks, and Public Spaces
   •   Green space and park areas: accounting for approximately 10–12% of the total project area;
   •   Including centralized parks, buffer green belts, and roadside trees along internal roads;
   •   These green spaces contribute to microclimate improvement, reduction of dust and noise,
       and provision of community spaces.
(iv) Other Social and Technical Infrastructure
   •   Internal road network and parking areas;
   •   Power supply, water supply, public lighting, and fire prevention and firefighting systems;
   •   Land reserved for small-scale public facilities serving the resettlement community.
(v) Public Utilities and Services
   •   Healthcare facilities: There are healthcare facilities located in the vicinity of the Project,
       including Pha Le Health Station, approximately 300 m to the northeast of the 8.56-ha
       resettlement area, and Lap Le Health Station, approximately 2 km to the southwest of the
       5.09-ha resettlement area. These facilities adequately meet the needs for primary
       healthcare services and community health care.
   •   Educational facilities: Within a radius of approximately 500 m from the Project area, there
       are sufficient educational institutions at all levels, including Lap Le Kindergarten, Pha Le
       Kindergarten, Lap Le Primary School, Pha Le Primary School, Lap Le Lower Secondary
       School, and Pha Le Lower Secondary School, providing convenient access to education
       for children of resettled households.
   •   Religious and belief facilities: The Project area is located close to community cultural and
       spiritual facilities, including Lap Le Pagoda, approximately 200–250 m to the southeast of
       the Project site, and Lap Le Parish Church, approximately 400 m to the south of the Project
       area.
   •   Commercial and service facilities: Lap Le Market, located approximately 250 m to the east
       of the Project area, serves as a hub for essential goods supply and adequately meets the
       daily livelihood needs of residents in the resettlement area.




                                                                                        P a g e | 102
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

                                                                                   P a g e | 103
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Overall, the location of the resettlement area in close proximity to existing healthcare, educational,
commercial, and cultural–religious facilities enables residents to easily access essential services,
facilitates the rapid stabilization of livelihoods following resettlement, and contributes to improving
quality of life while ensuring harmonious and sustainable development of the local community.
3. RELATIONSHIP WITH HPRDP
   •    The Nam Trieu Resettlement Area has been planned, approved, and implemented
        independently of HPRDP;
   •    HPRDP does not provide financing for the construction of the resettlement area;
   •    The use of the Nam Trieu Resettlement Area for HPRDP utilizes only part of the approved
        resettlement plots and does not result in additional construction items, expansion of scale,
        or changes to the approved design;
   •    Accordingly, the Nam Trieu Resettlement Area is considered a shared resettlement site,
        prepared and implemented in compliance with Vietnam’s national legal framework.
4. APPLICABLE LEGAL FRAMEWORK
The Nam Trieu Resettlement Project has been prepared and implemented in accordance with the
following key legal documents:
   •    Law on Public Investment;
   •    Land Law and its guiding decrees;
   •    Law on Environmental Protection (2020);
   •    Decree No. 08/2022/ND-CP;
   •    Decree No. 05/2025/ND-CP of the Government;
   •    Circular No. 02/2022/TT-BTNMT;
   •    Circular No. 07/2025/TT-BNNMT of the Ministry of Agriculture and Environment;
   •    National technical regulations on environment (QCVNs);

                                                                                          P a g e | 104
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

   •   Relevant regulations on planning, construction, fire prevention and firefighting, water supply
       and drainage, and waste management.
5. LEGAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL STATUS OF THE PROJECT
5.1 Investment Policy and Planning
   •   The project has been approved in principle by the Hai Phong City People’s Committee
       (Decision No. 3638/QD-UBND, dated 5 September 2025, issued by the Hai Phong City
       People’s Committee).
   •   The detailed master plan (scale 1/500) of the Nam Trieu Resettlement Area received a
       favorable opinion from the Hai Phong Department of Construction under Official Letter No.
       6029/SXD-QHKT dated 11 May 2025, confirming consistency with the approved master plan
       and zoning plans. The detailed planning has been duly approved pursuant to Decision No.
       2329/QD-UBND, dated 27 November 2025, issued by the Nam Trieu Ward People’s
       Committee.
5.2 Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA)
   •   The Environmental Impact Assessment (EIA) of the project was prepared in accordance with
       the Law on Environmental Protection (2020).
   •   The provincial-level EIA appraisal council convened the appraisal meeting in November
       2025, following receipt of the EIA dossier on 3 November 2025.
   •   Based on the appraisal comments, the project owner revised, supplemented, and submitted
       the completed EIA dossier on 4 December 2025.
   •   At the time of preparation of this DDR, the EIA dossier is under final administrative
       procedures for issuance of the approval decision by the competent authority.
From a legal and technical perspective, the EIA has completed all required steps under Vietnamese
law.
6. KEY ENVIRONMENTAL CONTENTS OF THE EIA
6.1 Environmental Baseline Conditions
   •   The current land use within the Project area mainly consists of double-cropped rice fields,
       interspersed with ponds, lakes, internal irrigation canals, existing transportation land, a
       number of small scattered structures, and individual graves.
   •   The project area does not fall within environmentally sensitive zones and does not include
       protected areas or endangered species;
   •   Air, water, and soil quality at the time of survey complied with applicable national standards.
6.2 Environmental Impacts and Mitigation Measures
Pre-construction Phase

The project will require acquisition of a total of 146,563.3 m² of land, affecting 460 affected
households and entities. The acquired land mainly comprises paddy land (127,769.5 m²), reflecting
the dominant land use pattern in the project area. In addition, the project will acquire 1,150.3 m² of
residential land with houses on land, and 17,323.1 m² of public land (including traffic land, irrigation
land and land with cultural houses). There are two (02) households affected by the acquisition of
residential lands with on-land houses, will need to be on-site relocated.

SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

To manage potential impacts during the pre-construction phase, the Project has allocated budget for
compensation, land acquisition, and resettlement, including compensation for land, structures, and
assets attached to land; assistance for relocation, livelihood stabilization, and livelihood restoration
through vocational training, job transition, and employment support, with the objective of ensuring
that affected households are able to maintain or improve their living standards compared to pre-
project conditions. Affected households will receive full compensation in accordance with applicable
regulations for agricultural land, crops, and livestock. For individual graves, financial assistance will
be provided to allow households to carry out relocation in accordance with their preferences.
To manage potential impacts during the pre-construction phase, the Project has allocated budget for
compensation, land acquisition, and resettlement, including compensation for land, structures, and
assets attached to land; assistance for relocation, livelihood stabilization, and livelihood restoration
through vocational training, job transition, and employment support, with the objective of ensuring
that affected households are able to maintain or improve their living standards compared to pre-
project conditions. Affected households will receive full compensation in accordance with applicable
regulations for agricultural land, crops, and livestock. For individual graves, financial assistance will
be provided to allow households to carry out relocation in accordance with their preferences.
With respect to the medium-voltage power line, the Project will implement relocation, removal of
utility poles, and undergrounding works, including the reuse or relocation of transformers in
accordance with the approved technical plan. Regarding the irrigation canals, the Project Owner
commits to reinstating or replacing affected irrigation infrastructure to ensure continued irrigation
and drainage for areas outside the Project boundary and adequate drainage for adjacent residential
areas. The scale and specific technical solutions will be calculated, described, and publicly disclosed
in subsequent project stages.
Unexploded ordnance (UXO): The Project Owner will enter into a contract with a licensed military
unit to carry out UXO clearance prior to the commencement of construction works.
Construction phase:
Key impacts include dust, noise, vibration, domestic wastewater from construction workers,
construction solid waste, and localized traffic safety risks.
Mitigation measures include dust suppression, covering of materials, appropriate construction
scheduling, proper waste collection and disposal, installation of barriers and warning signs, and
implementation of occupational safety measures.
                                                                                                 P a g e | 106
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Operation phase:
Main impacts include domestic wastewater, municipal solid waste, and potential odor risks from the
WWTP if not properly operated.
Mitigation measures include stable operation of the WWTP, organized solid waste collection through
municipal services, regular maintenance of technical infrastructure, and enhancement of green
spaces.
Overall environmental impacts are assessed as low to moderate and manageable.
6.3 Design Standards and Effluent Quality Requirements of the Wastewater Treatment Plant
   •   Design capacity: 300–450 m³/day;
   •   Type of wastewater: domestic wastewater;
   •   Effluent quality standard: QCVN 14:2025/BTNMT – Column A;
   •   Key controlled parameters include pH, BOD₅, COD, TSS, Ammonium, Total Nitrogen, Total
       Phosphorus, and Coliform.
The application of Column A as the design effluent standard demonstrates a high level of
environmental protection, ensuring safety for the receiving water body and consistency with Hai
Phong City’s sustainable urban development objectives.
7. COMMUNITY CONSULTATION AND STAKEHOLDER PARTICIPATION
Community consultation was conducted during the EIA preparation process in accordance with the
Law on Environmental Protection (2020).
   •   Consulted stakeholders: Nam Trieu Ward People’s Committee, the Fatherland Front and mass
       organizations, representatives of affected households, and local community members;
   •   Consultation methods: public meetings and disclosure of EIA information as required by law;
   •   Scale: more than 100 participants attended the consultation meetings (based on signed
       attendance lists included in the EIA dossier).
Consultation results:
   •   The majority of opinions supported project implementation;
   •   No significant objections were recorded;
   •   Comments mainly related to implementation schedule, infrastructure quality, and
       environmental management during construction;
   •   These comments were incorporated into the EIA.
Consultation with households affected by HPRDP continues to be conducted under the Stakeholder
Engagement Plan (SEP) and Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) of HPRDP.
8. OVERALL ASSESSMENT FOR HPRDP
The due diligence concludes that:
   •   The Nam Trieu Resettlement Area has been prepared and implemented in compliance with
       Vietnamese laws and regulations;
   •   No significant unaddressed environmental or social risks have been identified;
   •   The use of this resettlement area for HPRDP does not give rise to additional legal,
       environmental, or social obligations beyond those under national law.
9. CONCLUSION

                                                                                        P a g e | 107
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

The Nam Trieu Resettlement Area is an independently financed public investment project, not
using World Bank funds, and has been prepared and implemented in accordance with Vietnamese
legal requirements on investment, land, environment, and resettlement.
The resettlement area is legally and environmentally suitable for accommodating households
affected by the Hai Phong Urban Development and Flood Resilience Project (HPRDP) and meets
the World Bank’s requirement regarding compliance with the national legal framework.

                                                                                 P a g e | 116
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

ANNEXES 2. Summary Report on IOL and SES Results
1. Methodologies for Preparation of the RP
i)     Data collection:
         o Collect land use maps and cadastral maps of the project area: data sources include (i) the
             land acquisition boundaries of each investment item as supplied by feasibility study
             consultants (FS consultants); (ii) the cadastral map of the project area; and (iii) the
             extracted map managed by the cadastral officer of commune.
         o Collect contracts for the transfer of land use rights and ownership of assets attached to
             land in the project area or surrounding area to determine replacement costs.
ii)      The map overlay approach uses a land acquisition boundary layer (provided by the technical
consultants) and the most recent cadastral map of the project area to construct a list of impacted
households and assess the project's impact. This method ensures quick and reasonably accurate
statistics on the number of affected households, the number of individuals living in a single house,
the land types acquired, and the current land use conditions. The digitized database classifies
households as vulnerable. The digitized database determines the extent of occupancy and the
magnitude of the land acquisition's impact. Cadastral maps, which are updated annually, serve as the
legal basis for land use and management in the project area. Based on the above list of affected
households and level of impact, the Consultant worked with the neighborhood/hamlet heads and
cadastral officers to assess the inventory of losses (IOL).
iii)   The quantitative methods through a questionnaire was used to interview affected people
randomly selected from the list of affected households (household survey). The surveys collected
information from households on (i) demographic characteristics; (ii) occupation; (iii) living standards
(income, expenditure, bank debt, health situation, environmental sanitation, water supply); (iv)
production conditions; (v) social network; (vii) access to public services; (viii) vulnerability of
affected households; (ix) ability to restore livelihoods; (x) selection of resettlement options (if any)
and (xi) support for the project implementation. For the household survey using a questionnaire, a
sample comprising 1,708 affected households participated in the IOL questionnaire survey, and 463
households were selected from the 1,708 affected households to participate in the household socio-
economic survey, accounting for 31%.
iv)     The qualitative methods included (i) focus group discussions, (ii) key informant interviews,
and (iii) field observations. The qualitative methods are applied to gain an in-depth understanding of
the potential impacts of the projects on specific affected households, such as (i) displaced households
and/or households losing more than 30% of their productive land; (ii) households with production
and business activities; and (iii) households with land acquired. 11 Focus Group Discussions (FGDs)
were conducted in 11 wards, focusing on livelihoods, social impacts, gender, and vulnerable groups.
Key Informant Interviews (KIIs): 11 in-depth interviews were conducted. Target groups included:
severely affected households, vulnerable households, representatives of local authorities, business
households along the wastewater collection and drainage routes, and fishing households on the Re
River.
v)     Information disclosure and public consultation, such as rapid assessments and
consultations with stakeholders, on-site interviews and meetings with households, community
meetings, group discussions and social and economic surveys. A total of 16 consultation meetings
were organized in 11 out of 13 wards within the Project area (remaining Thuy Nguyen and Ngo
Quyen wards will be organized during the later stage due to the absence of residential populations),
with 1,917 participants (1,145 male and 772 female). Regarding online consultation, the Project
Owner issued Official Letter No. 1593/BQL-DA1 dated 01 October 2025 to the Ministry of Natural
                                                                                            P a g e | 117
SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

Resources and Environment (MONRE) to conduct consultation through public disclosure on the
electronic information portal for a period of 15 days, in accordance with regulations.
vi)     The Replacement Cost Survey (RCS) method was used to calculate the compensation unit
price for all assets affected by the project to meet the requirements of ESS5. Replacement costs
obtained through field surveys conducted by an independent valuation unit include (i) land prices
determined at the time of preparing the Resettlement Plan; (ii) prices of trees and livestock
determined at the present time; and (iii) prices for materials, workers, housing, and structures
specified at the present time. Regarding replacement costs, 10 information forms were collected in
each ward with land acquisition activities, comprising: 03 forms from households with affected land;
03 from households without land acquired by the project; 03 from construction material suppliers;
and 01 from a village/residential group official.
1.     In addition to the methodologies indicated above, tools such as Microsoft Office, SPSS,
Microstation, Autocad, Hhmaps, and GIS were utilized to process and analyze data and information
from field surveys, household interviews, and loss inventories to estimate the impact of land
acquisition the project.
2. Impacts on households and organizations
2.      It is estimated that approximately 2,675 households and organizations will be affected.
Among them, 2,652 households, equivalent to 10,171 people, will be permanently affected by the
project due to land recovery. Of these, 1,814 households (equivalent to 68.4%) will be severely
affected in their livelihoods/income (due to losing more than 30% of their total agricultural land area),
481 households have houses and architectural structures affected, and 207 households must relocate.
The project will also impact 175 vulnerable households and 51 business households. There are no
ethnic minority households affected, nor ethnic minority communities in the project area
3.     Table below shows the summary of HHs affected by 8 wards.

SOCIAL ASSESSMENT (SA)

3. Impacts on land
4.     According to the IOL, the project will permanently recover a total area of 1,973,885 m2 of
various types of land from 2,542 households and 23 organizations, including:
      − Land for annual crops is 633,360 m2 (1,910 households and 4 organizations);
      − Land for aquaculture is 227,562 m2 (40 households and 2 organizations);
      − Land for Perennial crop is 12,517 m2 (22 households);
      − Affected non-agricultural land area of 1,084,151 m2, including: Landresidential is 56,797 m2
      (351 households and 1 organization); non-agricultural production and business land of 66,630
      m2 (7 organizations); public service works land of 2,614 m2 (5 organizations); specialized water
      surface land of 653,310 m2 (8 organizations); transportation works land of 297,782 m2 (9
      organizations); religious establishment land of 1,5022 m2 (3 organizations);
      − Unused land area of 3,491 m2 (3 organizations)
4. Impacts on assets on land
a.         Affected structures:
5.      The damage inventory (IOL) results show that 481 households have affected houses, among
which 4 households built houses on land not designated as residential land. Among these, 95
households have temporary houses, 265 households have one-story houses, 104 households have
two-story houses, and 17 households have three- to four-story houses. The total affected house area
is 28,379 m2, with an average area of 59 m2 per house
6.     Other affected architectural structures include kitchens, yards, fences, gates, ancestral houses,
and graves. In addition, other household assets that may also be affected include electricity/water
meters, and water supply and drainage pipes.
                                  Table 49: Summary of affected structures
                                                                                                  Clan
Component        Ward      Unit House      Yard     Other construction Fence   Toilet   Kitchen         Grave
