5. Trees and crops:
7.     According to the inventory results, approximately 22,188 trees of various types belonging to
23 households and 8 organizations will be affected due to land recovery, including: 21,567 fruit trees
(mango, jackfruit, sapodilla, orange) and 621 timber trees, and 292,028 m² of crops affected
6. Impacts on business
8.      According to the IOL survey results, there are 51 households affected in business activities
across both components of the project. Among them, the highest concentration is in Bạch Đằng Ward,
An Dương Ward, and An Hải Ward.
7. Impact on vulnerable households and ethnic minorities
9.     According to the inventory results, there are 175 households belonging to vulnerable groups,
and no ethnic minority households reside in the project area.
8. Public assets and cultural heritages
10.     According to the damage inventory results, the land recovery area of the project partially
affects one pagoda, one worship shrine, and one communal house. During the design process, the
design consultant and the Project Management Board worked with the local authorities to avoid
relocating these structures
9. Socio-economic information of affected households
9.1 General information
11.    A sociological survey was conducted with 463 affected households (corresponding to 1,660
persons) in the area of 08 wards.
12.     Data related to affected households was collected from a socio-economic survey conducted
in the project area. The main objective of this section is to provide an overview of the socio-economic
conditions of the AHs, including demographic characteristics, income sources, living conditions,
infrastructure and sanitation, poverty status, etc. This information can be used as baseline data for
monitoring and evaluation during project implementation.
9.2. Household size
13.     The socio-economic survey was conducted covering a total of 463 households. The survey
results regarding average household size show that: The Dong Hai area has the largest household size
with an average of 6.0 persons/household; however, it should be noted that the sample size here is
very small (only 3 households), resulting in low representativeness. Nam Trieu follows with an
average of 5.05 persons/household (a large sample of 98 households), indicating that this is an area
with a tradition of large or multi-generational families.
14.    Smallest: The An Duong area has the smallest household size, with an average of 3.89
persons/household.
15.     The number of members in a household varies significantly, ranging from single-person
households (1 person) in Nam Trieu, Bach Dang... to large-scale households (11 people) in Nam
Trieu. The number of household members is determined by the total number of individuals living
together in a household at the time of the survey. The results indicate that the average household size
is approximately 4.6 persons per household, reflecting the common family living patterns in the study
area. Specifically:

9.3 Age of household heads
16.    A total of 463 household heads participated in the survey. Overall Mean Age: The average
age of household heads is 62 years. This indicates that the household heads in the survey sample are


                                                                                                P a g e | 123

and the oldest is 92 years old.

    household heads in the sample.
-   Lowest: Bach Dang (57.4 years) – An area with household heads younger than the general
    average.
-   Remaining areas: Fluctuating around 59 – 63 years (An Duong: 59.6; Dong Hai: 62.7; Hoa Binh:

9.4 Age of surveyed household members (excluding household heads)
17.     The total number of surveyed households is 463 (based on household head data). The total
number of recorded household members is 1,660 people. The average age of all household members
is 31.7 years; meanwhile, the average age of household heads is significantly high, reaching 62.0
years. The 30-year gap between the overall average age and the age of household heads indicates that
the multi-generational family model ('Three generations under one roof') remains very prevalent. The
elderly hold the main decision-making role (household heads), while the younger generation lives
together to provide care or remains dependent.
18.     Among all household members, females have a significantly higher average age than males
(Female: 35.3 years vs. Male: 27.4 years), suggesting that women's life expectancy is higher than
that of men in the project implementation area.
19.    Detailed information on the age distribution of household members is presented in the table
below.

9.6. Educational background of household heads
21.     The survey results indicate that respondents with lower secondary education account for the
largest proportion at 43.20%,concentrated mainly in Nam Trieu, Bach Dang, and Hoa Binh wards.
This followed by those who have completed upper secondary school at 25.92% concentrated in An
Duong and Bach Dang. The rates for primary education and university/post-graduate education are
15.55% (concentrated in Nam Trieu and Hoa Binh) and 6.48%, respectively. Additionally, illiterate
household heads account for only 2.16% primarily found in Bach Dang and Nam Trieu. The
education level of the household head also influences the economic conditions, division of labor, and
other information within the household. Detailed information on the education level of household
heads is presented in the table below.

9.8 Occupations of household heads
23.     The survey results indicate that among the 463 interviewed households, household heads
engaged in agricultural production account for the highest proportion at 46%. This is followed by
homemakers and retirees, ranking second at 35.0% – a figure that accurately reflects the age statistics
of household heads mentioned above. Next are freelance laborers (hired labor, casual work, etc.) at
12.31%, trading and services at 10.8%, fishing at 5.1%, and those with loss of working capacity at
6.05%. Additionally, other occupations account for small proportions, such as livestock breeding,
seafood processing, small-scale trading, retirement, housekeeping, or working for private companies.
Details on the occupations of household heads in each commune are presented in the table below.

9.12. Household’s domestic water sources
27.     Clean water is one of the criteria used to evaluate the development level and civilization of
urban residents. In the survey on water sources, the majority of surveyed households are using tap
water, with 455 households, accounting for 98.27%. There are 4 households (0.86%) stating they still
use drilled/dug well water, and 1 household (0.22%) still uses water from the irrigation system for
domestic purposes.

28.    Relying on a single water source can pose high risks if that source is interrupted or encounters
an incident, leading to difficulties in daily activities. However, along with the increasing speed of
urbanization, the issue of water security, supply, and assurance of clean water is always a top priority;
therefore, the trend of using a single water source in urban areas is increasing.
29.    Regarding production activities, most respondents stated that they use water from the clean
water supply system with 279 households (60.26%); 76 households (16.41%) use water from rivers
and canals for production; and 67 households (14.47%) use rainwater for production activities.

9.13. Living environment of residents in the project area
31.      Regarding common local diseases, cold/flu (47.51%) and other diseases (cardiovascular,
liver, kidney, cancer, etc.) (25.1%) are the most prevalent. Other diseases such as cholera, dysentery,
allergies, and diarrhea appear with nearly equivalent rates.

40.    The above data in the area shows a clear disparity, meaning that most households have income
at average or low levels, while a few households have very high income that pulls up the average.
The median income reaches 3.75 million VND per person, lower than the average of 4.71 million
VND per person, indicating significant disparity between households. The median being lower than
the average shows that most households have income below the average level. Specifically, the
lowest 20% of households only achieve under 2.88 million VND per person, while the highest 20%
of households exceed 5.33 million VND per person.
41.    According to urban near-poor household standards, 20% of the surveyed households have
income at the near-poor threshold, and the median indicates that most households exceed the urban
poverty standard.
42.     Income Sources: Many affected households have more than one income source. Households
with salary income account for a high proportion of 329/463 households. Next are income from
agricultural production activities and trading/services. Among households with agricultural income
sources, most have additional income sources. SES survey results show that among 243 households
with agricultural income, 225 households have income from other activities such as salaries,
trading/business/services, and other sources.

43.    Expenditure: The majority of household expenditures focus on basic family living needs,
including living expenses (100% of households), education expenses (53.66% of households),
medical expenses (76.72% of households), and ceremonial expenses (83.59% of households).
Notably, households show considerable attention to social and community activities, evidenced by
83.59% of households reporting monthly expenditures for such purposes. Other specific expenditures
account for low proportions, such as house repair/construction (12.64% of households),
production/business costs (25.72% of households), and other expenses (0.67% of households).
9.16. Household borrowing needs
44.      Borrowing capital from credit institutions plays an important role in supporting households
to achieve production and business goals, especially in the fields of agriculture and aquaculture.
Access to credit provides households with financial resources to purchase materials, expand
production scale, invest in infrastructure, or apply new technologies to improve productivity. The
survey results show that only 9.29% of surveyed households have borrowed capital from credit
institutions. Among them, 30.23% of borrowers used the funds for investment in trading/services,
27.91% borrowed for construction and house repair, 18.6% invested in education, and notably, up to
27.91% of respondents stated that the purpose of the loan was for medical examination and treatment.
Regarding loan sources, households mainly borrowed from relatives, friends, and neighbors,
accounting for 46.51%; other channels such as the Vietnam Bank for Agriculture and Rural
Development (Agribank) and the Vietnam Bank for Social Policies (VBSP) accounted for 16.28%
and 11.63%, respectively; borrowing from other banks accounted for 25.58%, and borrowing from
moneylenders accounted for only 2.33%.

9.17. Changes in Households’ living standards in the last 3 years
45.    Hoa Binh Commune is an area requiring special attention in the Resettlement Action Plan
(RAP) as it has the highest number and percentage of vulnerable households (16 households,
accounting for 15.84%). These are particularly concentrated in the group of elderly people living
alone (6 out of the 7 cases in the entire project are located here) and social policy households (8
households). Nam Trieu Commune ranks second in terms of the number of vulnerable households (9
households), mainly consisting of social policy households and female-headed households.
46.    Attention should be paid to the case of the single near-poor household under the project,
located in Bach Dang Ward. Specific income restoration measures are needed for this household to
prevent the risk of falling back into poverty.
47.    Regarding Ethnic Minorities: No ethnic minority households were recorded in any of the
surveyed areas.

50.       Survey results show that all affected households now have access to the national grid.
51.     Cooking fuels are diverse, with two main sources being electricity and gas. Only a very small
fraction (2 households) uses firewood or straw as cooking fuel. Specifically, 367 households use gas,
and 93 households use electricity for cooking activities.
9.20. Legal Status of Land
52.     For households, land and housing are not only places of residence but also valuable assets;
therefore, the Land Use Rights Certificate serves as the legal basis to prove the legality of citizens'
land use. For affected households, the Land Use Rights Certificate is an important basis and legal
foundation for determining compensation value as well as support amounts for the households.

9.21. Gender issues
57.    In order to understand the division of labor by gender, it has been conducted interview with
households by questions related to who will be mainly responsible for tasks such as shopping,
cooking, and taking care of children.… in the family. It will consider different duties and
responsibilities between men and women, who will do, what will they do, when will they do, how
and how long etc. and social recognition of labor. The division of tasks on the basis of gender is
known and understood by all members of each community/society.
58.    Women in the family not only have to be responsible for production work, but also carry the
burden of jobs that are considered as " odd jobs", making them always "busy". The progressing of a
community is measured by the progressing of women in that community. And the progressing of
women is reflected in whether they are "liberated", shared housework in the family and participated
in the common work of society? In general, women must fully perform the following tasks: (i)
Production work (production of goods and services for household consumption or income generation)
(ii) Reproduction work (giving birth, taking care of children, doing housework and maintaining
                                                                                                                P a g e | 143
                                                                                                                P a g e | 143
                                                                                                                      Official Use Only
family life) (iii) Community work (providing and maintaining resources used by people such as
water, health care, education, leadership participation). Men tend to participate in community and
production work.
59.     Regarding the division of labor in the region, there is no difference compared to other studies
and gender-based division of labor analyses in Vietnam: women participate in production,
reproduction, and childcare/family care activities, while men mainly participate in production tasks
and attend meetings at the commune and residential group levels.

60.    Several family issues are decided by both men and women. The rate of respondents stating
that both husband and wife make decisions together is higher than those stating only one person
(husband or wife) decides. Specifically:
   -     Regarding financial issues, the response "both husband and wife" accounts for 68.9%, while

   -     Regarding investment in production, the rate for "both husband and wife" is 71.49%,

ANNEXES 3. Rapid assessment of livelihoods and business activities
          RAPID ASSESSMENT OF LIVELIHOODS AND BUSINESS ACTIVITIES
I. SCOPE OF THE SURVEY
a. Survey scope
Pursuant to discussions between the Project Management Unit (PMU) and the technical design
consultant, the subprojects will not necessitate temporary land acquisition for constructing project
components. During project implementation, however, certain households engaged in business
activities within the construction zones of Component 1 and Component 3 will experience partial
disruptions. To mitigate these effects, construction will employ segmented approaches, nocturnal
operations, sequential phasing, and progressive site restoration. A dedicated project impact
assessment has evaluated the extent of these disruptions on affected households. Furthermore, should
the social impact assessment reveal substantial and prolonged income reductions for these
households, the project will provide commensurate financial assistance.
This report delineates the survey scope as encompassing wards within the construction areas of
Components 2 and 3. Investigations focused on prospective temporary impacts, including disruptions
to street-based vending (arising from pipeline and pump station works) and fishing livelihoods along
the Re River (stemming from embankment construction). Drawing on a comprehensive review and
evaluation of impact severity, the report proposes mitigation measures tailored to prevailing
conditions in Hai Phong City.




       Re River (area with fishing activities)    Blue and red lines: Sewer pipelines to be
                                                  installed along the streets.
            Figure 7. Estimated scope of temporary impacts on businesses and livelihoods
b. Survey on Temporary Livelihood/Business Impacts
The survey on temporary livelihood and business impacts—encompassing street-based activities
under Component 3 and fishing operations on the Re River due to river embankment works—
encompasses, but is not limited to, the following principal tasks:
   •    Reviewing extant Vietnamese regulations, policies, and guidelines on support mechanisms
        for business disruptions.
   •    Consulting representatives from Ward People's Committees, irrigation management agencies,
        and cadastral officers in affected wards regarding provisional construction impacts.
   •    Conducting field surveys of prevailing business conditions and infrastructure along streets
        targeted for drainage system upgrades, alongside fishing activities on the Re River.
   •    Assessing and proposing suitable replacement unit rates for compensation and support related
        to land and associated assets affected by the project.

                                                                                        P a g e | 147
                                                                                        P a g e | 147
                                                                                              Official Use Only
II. METHODOLOGIES FOR INVESTIGATION AND EVALUATION
a. Methods for Rapid Assessment of Impacts on Businesses and Livelihoods
   ➢ Qualitative Methods (In-depth Interviews)
   Target respondents: Owners of large enterprises, vulnerable business households (e.g., female-
   headed or elderly-led households), and vendors of relocation-challenged goods.
   Guiding questions:
           o   Have prior construction activities (e.g., road excavations or infrastructure works)
               disrupted your business operations or livelihoods?
           o   What specific impacts arose from those activities?
           o   What was the magnitude and duration of effects on your operations or livelihoods?
           o   What mitigation measures did you employ?
           o   Did you receive support from local authorities or contractors during implementation?
           o   What recommendations do you offer to the project owner and contractors for
               forthcoming activities?
   ➢ Key Informant Interviews (KII): Engaging functional agencies to validate data from local
     administrative perspectives on construction impacts to businesses and livelihoods, while
     eliciting recommendations and lessons learned.
   ➢ Field Observation: Undertaking site visits to document and photograph current business and
     livelihood conditions, enabling the consultant to quantify and classify potentially affected
     enterprises and activities.
b. Survey Results
      Drawing from direct stakeholder consultations, interviews with management agencies, and
local residents, the following synthesizes stakeholder observations, evaluations, and
recommendations concerning temporary impacts on business and livelihood activities under
Components 2 and 3:
c. Impacts on Re River Fishing
        Prior to the construction of the Cai Tack sluice, the Re River abounded in aquatic resources,
sustaining household livelihoods through tidal ingress of marine species into brackish and freshwater
zones. However, sluice installation since the 1980s—for river regulation purposes—has precipitated
a sharp decline in fish stocks, diminished resource quality, and reduced shrimp and fish sizes.
Presently, fishing involves approximately 20 small boats and constitutes only a supplementary
income source.
        In-depth interviews with Mr. T., a local boat operator, confirm that fishing serves as an
ancillary household activity, pursued primarily during idle periods from primary employment.
Current Re River fisheries yield predominantly small tilapia, with occasional catches exceeding 1 kg.
Larger specimens are sold for supplemental income, while smaller ones are retained for household
consumption or shared with kin and acquaintances. Mr. T. fishes approximately once or twice
weekly, underscoring the small-scale, non-primary nature of these operations. His activities have
proceeded uninterrupted in recent years, including during An Hai Company's recent riverbank
revetment works.
       The Re River, which also supplies domestic and irrigation water, undergoes routine
maintenance by its operator, including a current ~200 m upstream embankment project. With a wide
riverbed (50–60 m in segments), the ~10 m embankment footprint minimally impedes navigation or

                                                                                         P a g e | 148
                                                                                         P a g e | 148
                                                                                               Official Use Only
fishing. Phased construction further limits disruptions to small-scale local fishing in Hai Phong.
Officials from An Duong Ward affirm no obstructions to flows or navigation during ongoing
operations, maintenance, or embankment activities.
d. Impacts on Component 3 Affected Households' Temporary Business
        Construction activities along the culvert alignment will disrupt roadside businesses. The
HDPE D315–D500 collector line extends 2,600 m, with a total construction duration of
approximately 65 days using two work fronts. Mitigation employs nighttime construction and
daytime restoration via a segmented rolling method (20–30 m segments), with sequential piping
installation and terrain reinstatement. On dense or narrow roads, nighttime work includes daytime
coverage with 2 cm steel plates; wider, low-traffic roads permit daytime operations.
       Preliminary surveys of temporarily affected Component 3 businesses identify four categories:
(1) eateries, cafés, pharmacies, and lodging; (2) groceries and household goods; (3) beauty and
fashion services; and (4) consulting and banking services.
        In-depth interviews with affected business households revealed varied experiences. One
household selling steamed rice rolls (bánh cuốn) in mornings and evenings reported suspending
operations for excavation days during prior water supply pipe installation due to dust and access
difficulties, deterring customers. Business gradually normalized after initial days, but cumulative
closures spanned about ten days, yielding substantial income losses.
       Conversely, a nearby flower and coffee vendor experienced negligible disruption to flower
sales—attributable to location and product nature—and minimal revenue impact from indoor coffee
operations, as customer access persisted.
        Insights from prior projects employing analogous methods (road excavation for pipe laying)
indicate that segmented construction substantially curtails disruptions. Nonetheless, efficacy
demands rigorous adherence to temporary road restoration and backfilling protocols to sustain
customer access. Interviews with previously affected households affirm that the proposed plan—
short-segment rolling construction with rapid restoration—will limit cumulative full closures to
under half a month per household.

III. CONCLUSION
       Evidence from prior projects employing comparable construction techniques—such as road
excavation for pipe laying and river embankment works—demonstrates that segmented methods
substantially mitigate disruptions to business and livelihood activities.
        Effectiveness, however, hinges on stringent compliance with temporary road restoration and
backfilling measures to preserve customer access. In-depth interviews with previously affected
business and fishing households corroborate that the proposed plan—featuring short-segment rolling
construction and expedited restoration—will minimize impacts on commercial operations and
seasonal livelihoods.

ANNEXES 4. Project Information Booklet (PIB)
                 Project: Hai Phong City Resilience Development Project (HPRDP).
  I.      General Project Information:
      -   Project Name: Hai Phong City Resilience Development Project (HPRDP).
      -   Executing Agency (Line Agency): Hai Phong City People’s Committee (Hai Phong CPC).
      -   Project Owner (Implementing Agency): Hai Phong Project Management Unit for
          Investment in Construction of Transport and Agriculture Works.
      -   Financier: International Bank for Reconstruction and Development (IBRD) under the World
          Bank Group (WB).
      -   Project Location: Hai Phong City.
      -   Total Investment Capital: VND 9,908.17 billion, equivalent to USD 394.433 million, of
          which:
             1. WB IBRD Loan: VND 6,684.98 billion, equivalent to USD 266.121 million,
                accounting for 67.46% of the preliminary total project investment; to cover
                investment costs for construction items of Component 1, Component 2, Component
                3, and part of Component 4.
             2. Domestic Counterpart Fund: VND 3,223.18 billion, equivalent to USD 128.311
                million, accounting for 32.54% of the preliminary total project investment; to cover
                costs for compensation and site clearance, taxes, project management, construction
                investment consultancy, and other implementation costs during the project
                implementation process.
      -   Tentative Implementation Timeline:
             1. Project Preparation: 2023-2026 (from the approval of the Project Proposal until the
                signing of the ODA Loan Agreement).
             2. Project Implementation: 2026-2030 (Counting from the time capital is allocated by
                the competent authority).
II.       Project Objective
II.1. Overall Objective:
To foster the socio-economic development and growth of Hai Phong City and promote sustainable
urban development through a multi-sector integrated investment package focusing on urban
connectivity and regional linkage; ensuring environmental sustainability, water security, and urban
flooding reduction; adapting to regional climate change; upgrading public spaces and enhancing
urban resilience.
II.2. Specific Objectives:
      •   To reduce urban flooding and adapt to climate change for the inner city of Hai Phong through
          the construction, upgrading, and rehabilitation of the drainage system.
      •   To improve environmental conditions towards climate change adaptation, combining
          drainage, environmental sanitation improvement, spatial development, and the creation of
          urban landscapes; creating drivers for green development and advanced services; and
          improving the quality of life for residents in the inner city area, specifically along both banks
          of the Re River, and Hai Phong City in general.


                                                                                              P a g e | 152
                                                                                              P a g e | 152
                                                                                                    Official Use Only
       •   To enhance regional transport connectivity to create a driving force for the development of
           the Northwest - Southeast marine economic axis, promoting logistics development, aimed at
           building and developing Hai Phong into a regional linkage hub.
       •   To enhance the capacity for flood risk management and water quality control of river basins,
           ensuring safe and sustainable water supply for the city through construction, training, and
           technology transfer regarding intelligent management and monitoring systems; contributing
           to administrative reform and the formation and development of Hai Phong as a smart city.
III.       Project Implementation Scope
       •   Project Study Area: Includes the area on both sides of the Cam River extending to the Gia
           River in the North and the Lach Tray River in the South; the inter-provincial An Kim Hai
           river system to the East and the Bach Dang River to the West of Hai Phong City, covering an
           area of 193 km² (located in Thuy Nguyen City and the districts of Hong Bang and former An
           Duong). This includes research and general planning of contents according to project
           objectives, based on which specific construction investment items of the project will be
           phased to ensure investment efficiency, synchronization, and long-term viability.
       •   Construction Footprint of Project Items: Located within the administrative boundaries of
           Hong Bang, An Hai, An Duong, Gia Vien, Le Chan, An Bien, Ngo Quyen, Dong Hai, Thuy
           Nguyen, Nam Trieu, Bach Dang, Luu Kiem, and Hoa Binh wards of Hai Phong City, with an
           area of approximately 109 km².
Based on the specific objectives mentioned above, the Project is proposed to include 04 components.
The general layout of the Components and proposed project items is presented in the figure below.
1. Component 1: Integrated Development of Thuy Nguyen Urban Infrastructure Adapted to
Climate Change
This component aims to construct the Ring Road 3 route connecting Hai Phong City's beltway
transport infrastructure with regional transport and the Thuy Nguyen area, and to synchronously build
urban infrastructure to minimize environmental pollution, avoid traffic congestion, and save travel
time.
       •   Ring Road 3 Overview:
              o   Southern Start Point: Km0+00, at the intersection between Bui Vien Road (WB
                  Road) and Lien Phuong Road, connecting to the Hanoi - Hai Phong Expressway at
                  station Km104+200 (Hanoi - Hai Phong Expressway chainage), within Ngo Quyen
                  ward.
              o   End Point: Km18+680 at the intersection with National Road 10 (Km13+700 - NR10
                  chainage), within Hoa Binh ward.
              o   Alignment: From the Hanoi - Hai Phong Expressway, the route crosses the Cam River
                  (via Ngo Quyen Bridge) to Thuy Nguyen, continues across the Ruot Lon River,
                  follows the Eastern boundary of the VSIP area and along the Hau Long River, meets
                  Provincial Road 359 (at approx. Km10+906 - PR.359 chainage), then turns West and
                  continues straight to the end point at the intersection with NR 10.
              o   Scale: The total length of the entire route is approximately 18.65 km, including about
                  2.67 km of bridges crossing the Cam River and Ruot Lon River. Regarding scale and
                  classification, according to the approved planning, Ring Road 3 is classified as a Main
                  Urban Street (according to Table 5, TCVN 13592-2022), with a cross-section width
                  of 68m, including a 44m roadway (2x15m main route + 2x7m service roads), a 9m

                                                                                            P a g e | 153
                                                                                            P a g e | 153
                                                                                                  Official Use Only
               median strip (including a 5m center median and 2.0m separators between the main
               route and service roads on each side), and 7.5m sidewalks on each side.
           o   Phasing: However, in the immediate phase, investment will be carried out in phases
               (full site clearance, but service roads not yet constructed) with a Phase 1 cross-section
               scale as follows:
                  ▪   Main route roadway: 2x10.5m = 21.0m
                  ▪   Center median strip: = 5.0m
                  ▪   Shoulders on both sides: 2x0.5m = 1.0m
                  ▪   Total roadbed width: = 27.0m
2. Component 2: Re River Environmental Upgrade
This component aims to ensure freshwater security, improve water quality and environmental
sanitation of the Re River, enhance stormwater drainage management, prevent saline intrusion, and
adapt to climate change and sea-level rise. At the same time, it combines synchronous investment in
roads and infrastructure on both sides of the Re River to connect with transport along the river,
creating landscape and a public green space axis for the area from An Duong to Hong Bang.
a. Sub-component 2.1: Re River Infrastructure Upgrade
   •   Construction of the road along the Re River:
           o   The Re River is approximately 9.5km long, starting from Re 2 Bridge (intersection
               with National Road 10) to the end point at Cai Tat Sluice. The river width is
               approximately (40÷110)m. This sub-component will upgrade the existing road along
               the Re River bank and build a new management road along both sides of the river
               while creating space to arrange the wastewater collection system.
           o   Scale: The route has a cross-section right-of-way (ROW) width of approximately
               11.0m; except for areas with existing residential settlements close to the riverbank,
               where the ROW is 3.5m. This includes a 3.5m roadway, a 2.0m sidewalk adjacent to
               the river, and a 2.0m opposite sidewalk + earth slope. The total length of the newly
               invested management road along the river is approximately 13.77 km.
   •   Upgrading existing embankments and constructing additional river embankments in
       other areas:
           o   Upgrade, construct new embankments, and rehabilitate existing embankments with a
               total length of approximately 9.04 km, of which some sections use vertical
               embankments to create sufficient space for arranging wastewater collection
               infrastructure (as mentioned above).
b. Sub-component 2.2: Re River Basin Environmental Sanitation Upgrade
   •   Wastewater Collection System: Construct a system of interceptor sewers collecting
       wastewater on both sides of the Re River with 14.0 km of gravity sewers, 4.3 km of pressure
       sewers, and 12 wastewater pumping stations along the Re River management road and
       transmission pipelines leading to the An Duong 1 Wastewater Treatment Plant (WWTP),
       planned at Dong Thai commune, An Hai ward, adjacent to the left dike of the Lach Tray
       River; construct approximately 8.42 km of tertiary sewer network collecting household
       wastewater for synchronous connection to the wastewater collection sewer line.
   •   Wastewater Treatment Plant: Construct the An Duong 1 WWTP Phase 1 with a capacity
       of 10,000 m³/day. Treated wastewater meeting Type A standards according to

                                                                                           P a g e | 154
                                                                                           P a g e | 154
                                                                                                 Official Use Only
       QCVN14:2025/BTNMT will be discharged into the Lach Tray River located adjacent to the
       treatment area.
   •   Equipment: Provision of equipment for urban flood risk management systems, water quality
       management, and air environment management for areas within the project investment scope.
3. Component 3: Central Urban Flooding Reduction
This component aims to enhance storage and regulation capacity, reduce flooding, control water
levels, and improve the water environment of Tam Bac River/Lake, creating conditions for public
space development, urban redevelopment, embellishment of historical cultural values, and a modern,
attractive urban area. Main items include:
   •   Regulator Sluices: Construction of 01 regulator sluice at Tam Bac Bridge with Thuong Ly
       River and 01 regulator sluice at Thuy Doi Point at the mouth of Tam Bac River flowing into
       Cam River, forming a new Tam Bac River retention lake. Within the dam body of the
       regulator sluice at Thuy Doi Point, a stormwater pumping station with a capacity of 12 m³/s
       will be built to pump rainwater from the Tam Bac River lake out to the Cam River outside
       the urban area. The Tam Bac River lake combined with the existing Tam Bac lake creates a
       chain of retention lakes in the central urban drainage basin, with a total area of approximately
       14-15 ha.
   •   Connecting Culvert: Construction of a box culvert (WxH = 2.5x2.0m, Length L = 220m)
       connecting the existing Tam Bac Lake with the Tam Bac River lake. Construction and
       renovation of a number of drainage sewer lines to ensure corresponding capacity and enhance
       drainage capability, consisting of sewers with dimensions from WxH = 1.0x1.0m to WxH =
       3.0x2.0m, length 4.18km. The total length of stormwater drainage lines is approximately 4.40
       km.
   •   Wastewater Collection: Construction of a wastewater collection system including
       wastewater collection sewer lines and wastewater pumping stations from both sides of the
       Tam Bac River leading to Vinh Niem WWTP, with approximately 2.61 km of main
       wastewater collection sewers, diversion chambers (CSOs), 05 wastewater pumping
       stations, and renovation to upgrade the capacity of the existing Lan Be Pumping Station and
       tertiary sewers.
4. Component 4: System and Capacity Development for Climate Change Adapted Urban
Improvement
a. Sub-component 4.1: Enhancing Flood Risk Management Capacity Enhancing urban flood
management capacity. This sub-item will support the development of a system allowing technical
experts and decision-makers to better forecast and respond to floods through:
   •   Developing and calibrating a dynamic hydraulic model for flood forecasting and early
       warning scenarios for the areas of An Duong, Hong Bang, Thuy Nguyen, Le Chan, Ngo
       Quyen, and Dong Hai;
   •   Operating an integrated flood risk management system for the inner city area, including
       operating procedures for sluice gates and stormwater pumping stations when extreme
       conditions are forecast;
   •   Establishing a framework and information system to improve the Operation and
       Maintenance (O&M) of flood control infrastructure for the areas of An Duong, Hong Bang,
       Thuy Nguyen, Le Chan, Ngo Quyen, and Dong Hai; and



                                                                                          P a g e | 155
                                                                                          P a g e | 155
                                                                                                Official Use Only
   •   Providing training, workshops, and technical assistance to Hai Phong City officials to enhance
       flood risk management capacity, including the integration of Nature-based Solutions (NbS)
       into flood control infrastructure.
   •   This sub-component contributes directly to the project's objective of addressing the city's
       resilience risks and promoting the effectiveness of Components 1-3 of the project.
b. Sub-component 4.2: Enhancing Water Quality and Environmental Management Capacity
Proposal to establish air and water environmental monitoring systems at project investment items
and within Hai Phong City, connected to the Environmental Monitoring Center or the city's
environmental management agency, and operating procedures, including:
   •   Monitoring the quality and quantity of wastewater and effluent of the An Duong 1 WWTP
       and water quality at the Tam Bac sluice gate constructed under the project, connecting to the
       City's Environmental Monitoring Center;
   •   Monitoring air quality (H2S) of the An Duong 1 WWTP;
   •   Supporting the establishment of O&M procedures for the wastewater treatment system in the
       city to improve this activity.
   •   This sub-component contributes directly to the objectives of the Project and Components 2
       and 3.
c. Sub-component 4.3: Project Management Technical and financial resources provided by Hai
Phong City to ensure effective project implementation, construction quality, compliance with the
Bank's environmental and social standards, while ensuring transparency in procurement and high-
quality construction. Activities include the following work contents:
   •   Project Preparation Support: Surveys, Pre-Feasibility Study (Pre-FS) Report; Feasibility
       Study (FS) Report; Detailed planning for a number of project items; Other supporting reports
       serving WB appraisal.
   •   Project Implementation Support: Detailed design and preparation of Bidding Documents;
       Construction supervision services and other consulting services.
   •   Technical Assistance for Component 4: Establishing an early warning system including
       hydraulic models, GIS database, and establishing operating management and flood risk
       management procedures after constructing the early warning system; Supporting the
       finalization of water treatment system O&M procedures.
   •   Technical Assistance and other support services during the project implementation
       phase: Independent Audit; Independent Environmental and Social Monitoring to comply
       with WB social and environmental standards while still ensuring transparency in procurement
       bidding and high-quality construction; Post-project evaluation; and other technical support
       services. Training, workshops, and study tours; Propaganda and community awareness
       raising; and other technical support services.
   •   Project Management Support: Project implementation costs of the Project Management
       Unit (PMU); Training, workshops, and study tours; and other technical support services.


PROJECT GRIEVANCE REDRESS MECHANISM
The project’s GRM includes the following steps:
   •   Step 1 – Ward People's Committee: If a household or individual has a complaint, it may
       submit a written complaint or verbally inform the WPC's one-stop office. The officer in
       charge of this office is responsible for notifying the commune leaders of a resolution. The
                                                                                         P a g e | 156
                                                                                         P a g e | 156
                                                                                               Official Use Only
       WPC's will meet personally with the complainant. The time limit for settling complaints shall
       be 30 days from the time the complaints are received. The WPC's secretariat is responsible
       for documenting and filing all complaints within its jurisdiction.
   •   Step 2 – City People's Committee: If after 30 days, the aggrieved affected household does
       not hear from the PC of commune or if the affected household is not satisfied with the decision
       taken on his or her complaint, the affected household may bring the case, either in writing or
       in person, to the CPC. The CPC has 45 days to resolve the complaint. The CPC secretariat is
       responsible for maintaining records of complaints, actions, and outcomes. Complainants can
       also submit their complaints to the Court if they wish.
   •   Step 3 (final) - Administrative Court: If, after 45 days following the lodging of the
       complaint with the CPC, the aggrieved household does not hear from the CPC or if he or she
       is not satisfied with the decision taken on his or her complaint, the case may be brought to the
       Court for adjudication. The court's decision will be final.

                                          World Bank Office                          Services (GRS)

 •   The Nguyen Truong To project involves combined industrial and wastewater systems; the

ANNEXES 8. Community Health and Safety Action Plan
During the construction period, trucks and heavy vehicles may generate noise, dust, vibration, risks of flooding and traffic accidents, and temporarily affect
the quality of life of people living near the construction sites. Table 9.1 below presents potential impacts and mitigation measures during project
implementation.
                                                 Table 9.1: Potential Impacts during the Project Implementation
 No.        Impacts        Yes-Level        No                     Description of Impacts

 1     Noise and         Yes-medium                  Impacts: Noise arising from transport vehicles and construction machinery may affect the daily activities of residential
       vibration                                     areas, schools, and nearby structures. Vibration from construction activities may affect the structural integrity of adjacent
                                                     buildings.
                                                     Mitigation Measures: (i) Restrict construction activities at night (from 22:00 to 06:00) in residential areas; (ii) Construction
                                                     vehicles and machinery must have valid registration and undergo periodic maintenance; (iii) Limit the use of horns when
                                                     passing through residential areas; (iv) Adjacent structures must be insured to ensure compensation in case of damages
                                                     caused by construction activities.

 2      Air pollution    Yes-minor                   Impacts: Dust generated from transport vehicles, excavation, backfilling, material handling, and construction activities may
                                                     affect the daily lives of local residents and nearby agricultural production.
                                                     Mitigation Measures: (i) Material transport trucks must be securely covered with tarpaulin to prevent spillage; (ii) Spray
                                                     water on roads and construction sites during dry and windy days; (iii) Wash vehicle tires before leaving the construction
                                                     site.

 3     Disturbances of   Yes-Medium                  Impacts: Increased traffic density as construction materials (sand, stone, cement) will be sourced from local suppliers and
       rural traffic?                                transported directly to construction sites.
                                                     Mitigation Measures: (i) Implement appropriate traffic diversion; (ii) Avoid material transport during peak hours; (iii)
                                                     Deploy traffic controllers (flagmen) at construction site entrances/exits.

 4     Damage        or Yes- Medium                  Impacts: Heavy trucks transporting materials may cause degradation (potholes, subsidence) to existing inter-group and inter-
       deterioration of                              ward roads.
       urban roads                                   Mitigation Measures: (i) Strictly adhere to the load limits of existing roads; (ii) Contractors must commit to immediately
                                                     repairing/restoring any road sections damaged by construction activities to their original condition.

       digging, clearing                             if not managed properly.

ANNEXES 9. Participation Plan and Stakeholder Communication Strategy
1. Participation Plan
Consultation and participation of project-affected communities and civil society stakeholders are
conducted throughout the project concept, design, preparation, and implementation stages.
During the project design phase, the exercise involves fully sharing information on the Project with
the targeted beneficiary households, communities, and affected people in the 13 project wards. Its
purpose is to engage stakeholders on the potential impact and effectiveness of the Project to enhance
positive benefits for them as well as mitigate any negative impacts. It also serves to provide them
with information about projects and programs, including potential impacts, safeguard plans,
mitigation measures, and institutional arrangements.
The Participation Plan thus serves as a framework to guide the participation of civil society and
organizations during project implementation. The dissemination of project information to
stakeholders and affected communities and enabling dialogue on associated needs and preferences
can help reduce potential opposition to the project, avoid conflicts that may occur during
implementation, and minimize the risk of project delays.
The Participation Plan presented in Table 9.1 below identifies the key stakeholders assisting civil
society and representing the target beneficiaries and affected people under the project. It outlines the
reasons for their involvement, the mechanisms for participation, the parties responsible, and
indicative costs.

2. Stakeholder Communication Strategy (SCS)
The preparation and adoption of a stakeholder communication strategy ensure inclusiveness,
transparency, timeliness, and the meaningful participation of stakeholders in the project. The SCS
promotes selected messages targeted at key stakeholders consistent with established communication
objectives regarding what perspectives, actions, and changes should be promoted to ensure the
project’s success.
Objectives of SCS:
       •    To enhance project benefits and mitigate potential negative impacts through timely
            information on subproject components, particularly for the poor, women, and ethnic
            minorities (if any).
       •    To establish two-way information sharing/dialogue mechanisms with stakeholders.
       •    To communicate wastewater connection benefits, tariffs, and special provisions for poor and
            vulnerable households.
       •    To raise public awareness on environmental sanitation behavior and hygiene.
       •    To communicate the project’s Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) and procedures.
Resources and Responsibilities:
       •    PMU: Overall responsibility for updating and implementing the SCS.
       •    Women’s Union & Ward PCs: Assist in information dissemination and community
            mobilization.
